
  
BOULOGNE
SUR
MER
,
June
23
,
1763
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
You
laid
your
commands
upon
me
at
parting
,
to
communicate
from
time
to
time
the
observations
I
should
make
in
the
course
of
my
travels
and
it
was
an
injunction
I
received
with
pleasure
.
<EOS>
In
gratifying
your
curiosity
,
I
shall
find
some
amusement
to
beguile
the
tedious
hours
,
which
,
without
some
such
employment
,
would
be
rendered
insupportable
by
distemper
and
disquiet
.
  
<EOS>
You
knew
,
and
pitied
my
situation
,
traduced
by
malice
,
persecuted
by
faction
,
abandoned
by
false
patrons
,
and
overwhelmed
by
the
sense
of
a
domestic
calamity
,
which
it
was
not
in
the
power
of
fortune
to
repair
.
  
<EOS>
You
know
with
what
eagerness
I
fled
from
my
country
as
a
scene
of
illiberal
dispute
,
and
incredible
infatuation
,
where
a
few
worthless
incendiaries
had
,
by
dint
of
perfidious
calumnies
and
atrocious
abuse
,
kindled
up
a
flame
which
threatened
all
the
horrors
of
civil
dissension
.
  
<EOS>
I
packed
up
my
little
family
in
a
hired
coach
,
and
attended
by
my
trusty
servant
,
who
had
lived
with
me
a
dozen
of
years
,
and
now
refused
to
leave
me
,
took
the
road
to
Dover
,
in
my
way
to
the
South
of
France
,
where
I
hoped
the
mildness
of
the
climate
would
prove
favourable
to
the
weak
state
of
my
lungs
.
  
<EOS>
You
advised
me
to
have
recourse
again
to
the
Bath
waters
,
from
the
use
of
which
I
had
received
great
benefit
the
preceding
winter
:
<EOS>
but
I
had
many
inducements
to
leave
England
.
<EOS>
My
wife
earnestly
begged
I
would
convey
her
from
a
country
where
every
object
served
to
nourish
her
grief
:
I
was
in
hopes
that
a
succession
of
new
scenes
would
engage
her
attention
,
and
gradually
call
off
her
mind
from
a
series
of
painful
reflections
;
and
I
imagined
the
change
of
air
,
and
a
journey
of
near
a
thousand
miles
,
would
have
a
happy
effect
upon
my
own
constitution
.
<EOS>
But
,
as
the
summer
was
already
advanced
,
and
the
heat
too
excessive
for
travelling
in
warm
climates
<EOS>
,
I
proposed
staying
at
Boulogne
till
the
beginning
of
autumn
,
and
in
the
mean
time
to
bathe
in
the
sea
,
with
a
view
to
strengthen
and
prepare
my
body
for
the
fatigues
of
such
a
long
journey
.
  
<EOS>
A
man
who
travels
with
a
family
of
five
persons
,
must
lay
his
account
with
a
number
of
mortifications
;
and
some
of
these
I
have
already
happily
overcome
.
<EOS>
Though
I
was
well
acquainted
with
the
road
to
Dover
,
and
made
allowances
accordingly
,
I
could
not
help
being
chagrined
at
the
bad
accommodation
and
impudent
imposition
to
which
I
was
exposed
.
<EOS>
These
I
found
the
more
disagreeable
,
as
we
were
detained
a
day
extraordinary
on
the
road
,
in
consequence
of
my
wife
's
being
indisposed
.
  
<EOS>
I
need
not
tell
you
this
is
the
worst
road
in
England
with
respect
to
the
conveniences
of
travelling
,
and
must
certainly
impress
foreigners
with
an
unfavourable
opinion
of
the
nation
in
general
.
<EOS>
The
chambers
are
in
general
cold
and
comfortless
,
the
beds
paultry
,
the
cookery
execrable
,
the
wine
poison
,
the
attendance
bad
,
the
publicans
insolent
,
and
the
bills
extortion
;
there
is
not
a
drop
of
tolerable
malt
liquor
to
be
had
from
London
to
Dover
.
  
<EOS>
Every
landlord
and
every
waiter
harangued
upon
the
knavery
of
a
publican
in
Canterbury
,
who
had
charged
the
French
ambassador
forty
pounds
for
a
supper
that
was
not
worth
forty
shillings
.
<EOS>
They
talked
much
of
honesty
and
conscience
;
but
when
they
produced
their
own
bills
,
they
appeared
to
be
all
of
the
same
family
and
complexion
.
<EOS>
If
it
was
a
reproach
upon
the
English
nation
,
that
an
innkeeper
should
pillage
strangers
at
that
rate
;
it
is
a
greater
scandal
,
that
the
same
fellow
should
be
able
to
keep
his
house
still
open
.
<EOS>
I
own
,
I
think
it
would
be
for
the
honour
of
the
kingdom
to
reform
the
abuses
of
this
road
;
and
in
particular
to
improve
the
avenue
to
London
by
the
way
of
Kent
-
Street
,
which
is
a
most
disgraceful
entrance
to
such
an
opulent
city
.
<EOS>
A
foreigner
,
in
passing
through
this
beggarly
and
ruinous
suburb
,
conceives
such
an
idea
of
misery
and
meanness
,
as
all
the
wealth
and
magnificence
of
London
and
Westminster
are
afterwards
unable
to
destroy
.
<EOS>
A
friend
of
mine
,
who
brought
a
Parisian
from
Dover
in
his
own
post
-
chaise
,
contrived
to
enter
Southwark
after
it
was
dark
,
that
his
friend
might
not
perceive
the
nakedness
of
this
quarter
.
<EOS>
The
stranger
was
much
pleased
with
the
great
number
of
shops
full
of
merchandize
,
lighted
up
to
the
best
advantage
.
<EOS>
He
was
astonished
at
the
display
of
riches
in
Lombard
-
Street
and
Cheapside
.
<EOS>
The
badness
of
the
pavement
made
him
find
the
streets
twice
as
long
as
they
were
.
<EOS>
They
alighted
in
Upper
Brook
-
Street
by
Grosvenor
-
Square
;
and
when
his
conductor
told
him
they
were
then
about
the
middle
of
London
,
the
Frenchman
declared
,
with
marks
of
infinite
surprize
,
that
London
was
very
near
as
long
as
Paris
.
  
<EOS>
On
my
arrival
at
Dover
I
payed
off
my
coachman
,
who
went
away
with
a
heavy
heart
.
<EOS>
He
wanted
much
to
cross
the
sea
,
and
endeavoured
to
persuade
me
to
carry
the
coach
and
horses
to
the
other
side
.
<EOS>
If
I
had
been
resolved
to
set
out
immediately
for
the
South
,
perhaps
I
should
have
taken
his
advice
.
<EOS>
If
I
had
retained
him
at
the
rate
of
twenty
guineas
per
month
,
which
was
the
price
he
demanded
,
and
begun
my
journey
without
hesitation
,
I
should
travel
more
agreeably
than
I
can
expect
to
do
in
the
carriages
of
this
 
country
;
and
the
difference
of
the
expence
would
be
a
mere
trifle
.
<EOS>
I
would
advise
every
man
who
travels
through
France
to
bring
his
own
vehicle
along
with
him
,
or
at
least
to
purchase
one
at
Calais
or
Boulogne
,
where
second
-
hand
berlins
and
chaises
may
be
generally
had
at
reasonable
rates
.
<EOS>
I
have
been
offered
a
very
good
berlin
for
thirty
guineas
:
<EOS>
but
before
I
make
the
purchase
,
I
must
be
better
informed
touching
the
different
methods
of
travelling
in
this
country
.
  
<EOS>
Dover
is
commonly
termed
a
den
of
thieves
;
and
I
am
afraid
it
is
not
altogether
without
reason
,
it
has
acquired
this
appellation
.
<EOS>
The
people
are
said
to
live
by
piracy
in
time
of
war
;
and
by
smuggling
and
fleecing
strangers
in
time
of
peace
:
but
I
will
do
them
the
justice
to
say
,
they
make
no
distinction
between
foreigners
and
natives
.
<EOS>
Without
all
doubt
a
man
can
not
be
much
worse
lodged
and
worse
treated
in
any
part
of
Europe
;
nor
will
he
in
any
other
place
meet
with
more
flagrant
instances
of
fraud
,
imposition
,
and
brutality
.
<EOS>
One
would
imagine
they
had
formed
a
general
conspiracy
against
all
those
who
either
go
to
,
or
return
from
the
continent
.
<EOS>
About
five
years
ago
,
in
my
passage
from
Flushing
to
Dover
,
the
master
of
the
packet
-
boat
brought
-
to
all
of
a
sudden
off
the
South
Foreland
,
although
the
wind
was
as
favourable
as
it
could
blow
.
<EOS>
He
was
immediately
boarded
by
a
customhouse
boat
,
the
officer
of
which
appeared
to
be
his
friend
.
<EOS>
He
then
gave
the
passengers
to
understand
,
that
as
it
was
low
water
,
the
ship
could
not
go
into
the
harbour
;
but
that
the
boat
would
carry
them
ashore
with
their
baggage
.
  
<EOS>
The
custom
-
house
officer
demanded
a
guinea
for
this
service
,
and
the
bargain
was
made
.
<EOS>
Before
we
quitted
the
ship
,
we
were
obliged
to
gratify
the
cabin
-
boy
for
his
attendance
,
and
to
give
drink
-
money
to
the
sailors
.
<EOS>
The
boat
was
run
aground
on
the
open
beach
;
but
we
could
not
get
ashore
without
the
assistance
of
three
or
four
fellows
,
who
insisted
upon
being
paid
for
their
trouble
.
<EOS>
Every
parcel
and
bundle
,
as
it
was
landed
,
was
snatched
up
by
a
separate
porter
:
<EOS>
one
ran
away
with
a
hat
-
box
,
another
with
a
wig
-
box
,
a
third
with
a
couple
of
shirts
tied
up
in
a
handkerchief
,
and
two
were
employed
in
carrying
a
small
portmanteau
that
did
not
weigh
forty
pounds
.
<EOS>
All
our
things
were
hurried
to
the
custom
-
house
to
be
searched
,
and
the
searcher
was
paid
for
disordering
our
cloaths
:
from
thence
they
were
removed
to
the
inn
,
where
the
porters
demanded
half
-
a
-
crown
each
for
their
labour
.
<EOS>
It
was
in
vain
to
expostulate
;
they
surrounded
the
house
like
a
pack
of
hungry
bounds
,
and
raised
such
a
clamour
,
that
we
were
fain
to
comply
.
<EOS>
After
we
had
undergone
all
this
imposition
,
we
were
visited
by
the
master
of
the
packet
,
who
,
having
taken
our
fares
,
and
wished
us
joy
of
our
happy
arrival
in
England
,
expressed
his
hope
that
we
would
remember
the
poor
master
,
whose
wages
were
very
small
,
and
who
chiefly
depended
upon
the
generosity
of
the
passengers
.
<EOS>
I
own
I
was
shocked
at
his
meanness
,
and
could
not
help
telling
him
so
.
<EOS>
I
told
him
,
I
could
not
conceive
what
title
he
had
to
any
such
gratification
<EOS>
:
he
had
sixteen
passengers
,
who
paid
a
guinea
each
,
on
the
supposition
that
every
person
should
have
a
bed
;
but
there
were
no
more
than
eight
beds
in
the
cabin
,
and
each
of
these
was
occupied
before
I
came
on
board
;
so
that
if
we
had
been
detained
at
sea
a
whole
week
by
contrary
winds
and
bad
weather
,
one
half
of
the
passengers
must
have
slept
upon
the
boards
,
howsoever
their
health
might
have
suffered
from
this
want
of
accommodation
.
<EOS>
Notwithstanding
this
check
,
he
was
so
very
abject
and
importunate
,
that
we
gave
him
a
crown
a
-
piece
,
and
he
retired
.
  
<EOS>
The
first
thing
I
did
when
I
arrived
at
Dover
this
last
time
,
was
to
send
for
the
master
of
a
packet
-
boat
,
and
agree
with
him
to
carry
us
to
Boulogne
at
once
,
by
which
means
I
saved
the
expence
of
travelling
by
land
from
Calais
to
this
last
place
,
a
journey
of
four
-
and
-
twenty
miles
.
<EOS>
The
hire
of
a
vessel
from
Dover
to
Boulogne
is
precisely
the
same
as
from
Dover
to
Calais
,
five
guineas
;
but
this
skipper
demanded
eight
,
and
,
as
I
did
not
know
the
fare
,
I
agreed
to
give
him
six
.
<EOS>
We
embarked
between
six
and
seven
in
the
evening
,
and
found
ourselves
in
a
most
wretched
hovel
,
on
board
what
is
called
a
Folkstone
cutter
.
<EOS>
The
cabin
was
so
small
that
a
dog
could
hardly
turn
in
it
,
and
the
beds
put
me
in
mind
of
the
holes
described
in
some
catacombs
,
in
which
the
bodies
of
the
dead
were
deposited
,
being
thrust
in
with
the
feet
foremost
;
there
was
no
getting
into
them
but
end
-
ways
,
and
indeed
they
seemed
so
dirty
,
that
nothing
but
extreme
necessity
could
have
obliged
me
to
use
them
.
<EOS>
We
sat
up
all
night
in
a
most
uncomfortable
situation
,
tossed
about
by
the
sea
,
cold
,
arid
cramped
and
weary
,
and
languishing
for
want
of
sleep
.
<EOS>
At
three
in
the
morning
the
master
came
down
,
and
told
us
we
were
just
off
the
harbour
of
Boulogne
;
but
the
wind
blowing
off
shore
,
he
could
not
possibly
enter
,
and
therefore
advised
us
to
go
ashore
in
the
boat
.
<EOS>
I
went
upon
deck
to
view
the
coast
,
when
he
pointed
to
the
place
where
he
said
Boulogne
stood
,
declaring
at
the
same
time
we
were
within
a
short
mile
of
the
harbour
's
mouth
.
<EOS>
The
morning
was
cold
and
raw
,
and
I
knew
myself
extremely
subject
to
catch
cold
;
nevertheless
we
were
all
so
impatient
to
be
ashore
,
that
I
resolved
to
take
his
advice
.
<EOS>
The
boat
was
already
hoisted
out
,
and
we
went
on
board
of
it
,
after
I
had
paid
the
captain
and
gratified
his
crew
.
<EOS>
We
had
scarce
parted
from
the
ship
,
when
we
perceived
a
boat
coming
towards
us
from
the
shore
;
and
the
master
gave
us
to
understand
,
it
was
coming
to
carry
us
into
the
harbour
.
<EOS>
When
I
objected
to
the
trouble
of
shifting
from
one
boat
to
another
in
the
open
sea
,
which
(
by
the
bye
)
was
a
little
rough
;
he
said
it
was
a
privilege
which
the
watermen
of
Boulogne
had
,
to
carry
all
passengers
ashore
,
and
that
this
privilege
he
durst
not
venture
to
infringe
.
<EOS>
This
was
no
time
nor
place
to
remonstrate
.
<EOS>
The
French
boat
came
alongside
half
filled
with
water
,
and
we
were
handed
from
the
one
to
the
other
.
<EOS>
We
were
then
obliged
to
lie
upon
our
oars
,
till
the
captain
's
boat
went
on
board
and
returned
from
the
ship
with
a
packet
of
letters
.
<EOS>
We
were
afterwards
rowed
a
long
league
,
in
a
rough
sea
,
against
wind
and
tide
,
before
we
reached
the
harbour
,
where
we
landed
,
benumbed
with
cold
,
and
the
women
excessively
sick
:
from
our
landing
-
place
we
were
obliged
to
walk
very
near
a
mile
to
the
inn
where
we
purposed
to
lodge
,
attended
by
six
or
seven
men
and
women
,
bare
-
legged
,
carrying
our
baggage
.
<EOS>
This
boat
cost
me
a
guinea
,
besides
paying
exorbitantly
the
people
who
carried
our
things
;
so
that
the
inhabitants
of
Dover
and
of
Boulogne
seem
to
be
of
the
same
kidney
,
and
indeed
they
understand
one
another
perfectly
well
.
<EOS>
It
was
our
honest
captain
who
made
the
signal
for
the
shore
-
boat
before
I
went
upon
deck
;
by
which
means
he
not
only
gratified
his
friends
,
the
watermen
of
Boulogne
,
but
also
saved
about
fifteen
shillings
portage
,
which
he
must
have
paid
<EOS>
had
he
gone
into
the
harbour
;
and
thus
he
found
himself
at
liberty
to
return
to
Dover
,
which
he
reached
in
four
hours
.
<EOS>
I
mention
these
circumstances
as
a
warning
to
other
passengers
.
<EOS>
When
a
man
hires
a
packet
-
boat
from
Dover
to
Calais
or
Boulogne
,
let
him
remember
that
the
stated
price
is
five
guineas
;
and
let
him
insist
upon
being
carried
into
the
harbour
in
the
ship
,
without
paying
the
least
regard
to
the
representations
of
the
master
,
who
is
generally
a
little
dirty
knave
.
<EOS>
When
he
tells
you
it
is
low
water
,
or
the
wind
is
in
your
teeth
,
you
may
say
you
will
stay
on
board
till
it
is
high
water
,
or
till
the
wind
comes
favourable
.
<EOS>
If
he
sees
you
are
resolute
,
he
will
find
means
to
bring
his
ship
into
the
harbour
,
or
at
least
to
convince
you
,
without
a
possibility
of
your
being
deceived
,
that
it
is
not
in
his
power
.
<EOS>
After
all
,
the
fellow
himself
was
a
loser
by
his
finesse
;
if
he
had
gone
into
the
harbour
,
he
would
have
had
another
fare
immediately
back
to
Dover
,
for
there
was
a
Scotch
gentleman
at
the
inn
waiting
for
such
an
opportunity
.
  
<EOS>
Knowing
my
own
weak
constitution
,
I
took
it
for
granted
this
morning
's
adventure
<EOS>
would
cost
me
a
fit
of
illness
;
and
what
added
to
my
chagrin
,
when
we
arrived
at
the
inn
,
all
the
beds
were
occupied
;
so
that
we
were
obliged
to
sit
in
a
cold
kitchen
above
two
hours
,
until
some
of
the
lodgers
should
get
up
.
<EOS>
This
was
such
a
bad
specimen
of
French
accommodation
,
that
my
wife
could
not
help
regretting
even
the
inns
of
Rochester
,
Sittingbourn
,
and
Canterbury
:
bad
as
they
are
,
they
certainly
have
the
advantage
,
when
compared
with
the
execrable
auberges
of
this
country
,
where
one
finds
nothing
but
dirt
and
imposition
.
<EOS>
One
would
imagine
the
French
were
still
at
war
with
the
English
,
for
they
pillage
them
without
mercy
.
  
<EOS>
Among
the
strangers
at
this
inn
where
we
lodged
,
there
was
a
gentleman
of
the
faculty
,
just
returned
from
Italy
.
<EOS>
Understanding
that
I
intended
to
winter
in
the
South
of
France
,
on
account
of
a
pulmonic
disorder
,
he
strongly
recommended
the
climate
of
Nice
in
Provence
,
which
,
indeed
,
I
had
often
heard
extolled
;
and
I
am
almost
resolved
to
go
thither
,
not
only
for
the
sake
of
the
air
,
but
also
for
its
situation
on
the
Mediterranean
,
where
I
can
have
the
benefit
of
bathing
;
and
from
whence
there
is
a
short
cut
by
sea
to
Italy
,
should
I
find
it
necessary
to
try
the
air
of
Naples
.
  
<EOS>
After
having
been
ill
accommodated
three
days
at
our
inn
,
we
have
at
last
found
commodious
lodgings
,
by
means
of
Mrs
B-
,
a
very
agreeable
French
lady
,
to
whom
we
were
recommended
by
her
husband
,
who
is
my
countryman
,
and
at
present
resident
in
London
.
<EOS>
For
three
guineas
a
month
we
have
the
greatest
part
of
a
house
tolerably
furnished
;
four
bed
-
chambers
on
the
first
floor
,
a
large
parlour
below
,
a
kitchen
,
and
the
use
of
a
cellar
.
  
<EOS>
These
,
I
own
,
are
frivolous
incidents
,
scarce
worth
committing
to
paper
;
but
they
may
serve
to
introduce
observations
of
more
consequence
;
and
in
the
mean
time
I
know
nothing
will
be
indifferent
to
you
,
that
concerns
 
Your
humble
servant
.
   
<EOS>
BOULOGNE
SUR
MER
,
July
15
,
1763
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
The
custom
-
house
officers
at
Boulogne
,
though
as
alert
,
are
rather
more
civil
than
those
on
your
side
of
the
water
.
<EOS>
I
brought
no
plate
along
with
me
,
but
a
dozen
and
a
half
of
spoons
,
and
a
dozen
teaspoons
<EOS>
:
the
first
being
found
in
one
of
our
portmanteaus
,
when
they
were
examined
at
the
bureau
,
cost
me
seventeen
livres
entree
;
the
others
being
luckily
in
my
servant
's
pocket
,
escaped
duty
free
.
<EOS>
All
wrought
silver
imported
into
France
,
pays
at
the
rate
of
so
much
per
mark
:
therefore
those
who
have
any
quantity
of
plate
,
will
do
well
to
leave
it
behind
them
,
unless
they
can
confide
in
the
dexterity
of
the
shipmasters
;
some
of
whom
will
undertake
to
land
it
without
the
ceremony
of
examination
.
<EOS>
The
ordonnances
of
France
are
so
unfavourable
to
strangers
,
that
they
oblige
them
to
pay
at
the
rate
of
five
per
cent
.
<EOS>
for
all
the
bed
and
table
linen
which
they
bring
into
the
kingdom
,
even
though
it
has
been
used
.
<EOS>
When
my
trunks
arrived
in
a
ship
from
the
river
Thames
,
I
underwent
this
ordeal
:
but
what
gives
me
more
vexation
,
my
books
have
been
stopped
at
the
bureau
;
and
will
be
sent
to
Amiens
at
my
expence
,
to
be
examined
by
the
chambre
syndicale
;
lest
they
should
contain
something
prejudicial
to
the
state
,
or
to
the
religion
of
the
country
.
<EOS>
This
is
a
species
of
oppression
which
one
would
not
expect
to
meet
with
in
France
,
which
piques
itself
on
its
politeness
and
hospitality
:
but
the
truth
is
,
I
know
no
country
in
which
strangers
are
worse
treated
with
respect
to
their
essential
concerns
.
<EOS>
If
a
foreigner
dies
in
France
,
the
king
seizes
all
his
effects
,
even
though
his
heir
should
be
upon
the
spot
;
and
this
tyranny
is
called
the
droit
d'aubaine
founded
at
first
upon
the
supposition
,
that
all
the
estate
of
foreigners
residing
in
France
was
acquired
in
that
kingdom
,
and
that
,
therefore
,
it
would
be
unjust
to
convey
it
to
another
country
.
<EOS>
If
an
English
protestant
goes
to
France
for
the
benefit
of
his
health
,
attended
by
his
wife
or
his
son
,
or
both
,
and
dies
with
effects
in
the
house
to
the
amount
of
a
thousand
guineas
,
the
king
seizes
the
whole
,
the
family
is
left
destitute
,
and
the
body
of
the
deceased
is
denied
christian
burial
.
<EOS>
The
Swiss
,
by
capitulation
,
are
exempted
from
this
despotism
,
and
so
are
the
Scots
,
in
consequence
of
an
ancient
alliance
between
the
two
nations
.
<EOS>
The
same
droit
d'aubaine
is
exacted
by
some
of
the
princes
in
Germany
:
but
it
is
a
great
discouragement
to
commerce
,
and
prejudices
every
country
where
it
is
exercised
,
to
ten
times
the
value
of
what
it
brings
into
the
coffers
of
the
sovereign
.
  
<EOS>
I
am
exceedingly
mortified
at
the
detention
of
my
books
,
which
not
only
deprives
me
of
an
amusement
which
I
can
very
ill
dispense
with
;
but
,
in
all
probability
,
will
expose
me
to
sundry
other
inconveniencies
.
<EOS>
I
must
be
at
the
expence
of
sending
them
sixty
miles
to
be
examined
,
and
run
the
risque
of
their
being
condemned
;
and
,
in
the
mean
time
,
I
may
lose
the
opportunity
of
sending
them
with
my
heavy
baggage
by
sea
to
Bourdeaux
,
to
be
sent
up
the
Garonne
to
Tholouse
,
and
from
thence

through
the
canal
of
Languedoc
to
Cette
,
which
is
a
sea
-
port
on
the
Mediterranean
,
about
three
or
four
leagues
from
Montpelier
.
  
<EOS>
For
the
recovery
of
my
books
,
I
had
recourse
to
the
advice
of
my
landlord
,
Mons
B
 
.
<EOS>
He
is
a
handsome
young
fellow
,
about
twenty
-
five
years
of
age
,
and
keeps
house
with
two
maiden
sisters
,
who
are
professed
devotees
.
<EOS>
The
brother
is
a
little
libertine
,
good

and

;
but
a
true
Frenchman
in
vanity
,
which
is
undoubtedly
the
ruling
passion
of
this
volatile
people
.
<EOS>
He
has
an
inconsiderable
place
under
the
government
,
in
consequence
of
which
he
is
permitted
to
wear
a
sword
,
a
privilege
which
he
does
not
fail
to
use
.
<EOS>
He
is
likewise
receiver
of
the
tythes
of
the
clergy
in
this
district
,
an
office
that
gives
him
a
command
of
money
,
and
he
,
moreover
,
deals
in
the
wine
trade
.
<EOS>
When

came
to
his
house
,
he
made
a
parade
of
all
these
advantages
:
he
displayed
his
bags
of
money
,
and
some
old
gold
which
his
father
had
left
him
.
<EOS>
He
described
his
chateau
in
the
country
;
dropped
hints
of
the
fortunes
that
were
settled
upon
mademoiselles
his
sisters
;
boasted
of
his
connexions
at
court
;
and
assured
me
it
was
not
for
my
money
that
he
let
his
lodgings
,
but
altogether
with
a
view
to
enjoy
the

of
my
company
.
<EOS>
The
truth
,
when
stript
of
all
embellishments
,
is
this
:
<EOS>
the
sieur
B
  
is
the
son
of
an
honest
bourgeois
lately
dead
,
who
left
him
the
house
,
with
some
stock
in
trade
,
a
little
money
,
and
a

:
his

about

(
not

140
L
)
apiece
;
the

's

worth

,

or

's

,
with

.
<EOS>
My

piques

and

sex
:
<EOS>
he
keeps
a
fille

,
and

.
<EOS>
He
told
miss
C
  
<EOS>
the

,
in
broken

,
that
,

the

,

.
<EOS>
He

,

,

;

,

.
<EOS>
This
,

,

.
<EOS>
Yesterday

,

of

:
the

;

;

.
  
<EOS>
But

this
digression
:
<EOS>
Mr
B
  
advised

to

or

,
that

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
He

,

;

,

.
<EOS>
He

;

,

.
<EOS>
He

,

,

<EOS>
I

:

.
<EOS>
Meanwhile

,

,

<EOS>
Such

.
<EOS>
I

;

,

,

.
<EOS>
I

H
 
;
and

D
 
,

,

.
<EOS>
What

,

:
but
<EOS>
the

B
  
shakes

,

,

<EOS>
I

,

,

Your

BOULOGNE
,

15
,
1763
.
  
<EOS>
SIR
 
<EOS>
I
am
much
obliged
to
you
for
your
kind
enquiries
after
my

work
remain
;
what

,

.
<EOS>
I

<EOS>
On

<EOS>
Never

<EOS>
It

<EOS>
Besides

,

,

:
<EOS>
nay
,

<EOS>
The

,

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
It

,

.
<EOS>
As

,

,

,

;

.
<EOS>
On

,

,

.
<EOS>
He

,

.
  
<EOS>
In

,

,

.
<EOS>
This

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Be

,

.
<EOS>
Being

,

<EOS>
I

,

.
<EOS>
At

,

<EOS>
About

<EOS>
There

<EOS>
Some

<EOS>
In

<EOS>
If

<EOS>
A

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
He

<EOS>
The

"
Ah
,

!
<EOS>
(

)
<EOS>
I

you
<EOS>

<EOS>
We

.
<EOS>
He

.
<EOS>
Poor

!
<EOS>

<EOS>
I

.
"
  
<EOS>
Among
other
public

at
Boulogne
,
there
is
an
hospital
,
or

,
which
seems
to
be
established

.
<EOS>
It

,
who
are
kept

at

,
according

their

,
in

,
all

,
a

,

.
<EOS>
It

;
and

,

.
<EOS>
The

in
this

are
but

,

.
<EOS>
There

,

.
  
<EOS>
In

.
<EOS>
Mean

Yours
.
   
<EOS>
BOULOGNE
,
September
1
,
1763
.
  
<EOS>
SIR
,
 
I
am
infinitely
obliged
to
D
H
  
for
the
favourable
manner
in
which
he
has
mentioned
me
to
the
earl
of
H
  
<EOS>
I
have
at
last
recovered
my
books
,
by
virtue
of
a
particular
order
to
the
director
of
the
douane
,
procured
by
the
application
of
the
English
resident
to
the
French
ministry
.
<EOS>
I
am
now
preparing
for
my
long
journey
;
but
,
before
I
leave
this
place
,
I
shall
send
you
the
packet
I
mentioned
,
by
Meriton
.
<EOS>
Mean
<EOS>
-
while
I
must
fulfil
my
promise
in
communicating
the
observations
I
have
had
occasion
to
make
upon
this
town
and
country
.
  
<EOS>
The
air
of
Boulogne
is
cold
and
moist
,
and
,
I
believe
,
of
consequence
unhealthy
.
<EOS>
Last
winter
the
frost
,
which
continued
six
weeks
in
London
,
lasted
here
eight
weeks
without
intermission
;
and
the
cold
was
so
intense
,
that
,
in
the
garden
of
the
Capuchins
,
it
split
the
bark
of
several
elms
from
top
to
bottom
.
<EOS>
On
our
arrival
here
we
found
all
kinds
of
fruit
more
backward
than
in
England
.
<EOS>
The
frost
,
in
its
progress
to
Britain
,
is
much
weakened
in
crossing
the
sea
.
<EOS>
The
atmosphere
,
impregnated
with
saline
particles
,
resists
the
operation
of
freezing
.
<EOS>
Hence
,
in
severe
winters
,
all
places
near
the
sea
-
side
are
less
cold
than
more
inland
districts
.
<EOS>
This
is
the
reason
why
the
winter
is
often
more
mild
at
Edinburgh
than
at
London
.
<EOS>
A
very
great
degree
of
cold
is
required
to
freeze
salt
water
.
<EOS>
Indeed
it
will
not
freeze
at
all
,
until
it
has
deposited
all
its
salt
.
<EOS>
It
is
now
generally
allowed
among
philosophers
,
that
water
is
no
more
than
ice
thawed
by
heat
,
either
solar
,
or
subterranean
,
or
both
;
and
that
this
heat
being
expelled
,
it
would
return
to
its
natural
consistence
.
<EOS>
This
being
the
case
,
nothing
else
is
required
for
the
freezing
of
water
,
than
a
certain
degree
of
cold
,
which
may
be
generated
by
the
help
of
salt
,
or
spirit
of
nitre
,
even
under
the
line
.
<EOS>
I
would
propose
,
therefore
,
that
an
apparatus
of
this
sort
should
be
provided
in
every
ship
that
goes
to
sea
;
and
in
case
there
should
be
a
deficiency
of
fresh
water
on
board
,
the
seawater
may
be
rendered
potable
,
by
being
first
converted
into
ice
.
  
<EOS>
The
air
of
Boulogne
is
not
only
loaded
with
a
great
evaporation
from
the
sea
,
increased
by
strong
gales
of
wind
from
the
West
and
South
-
West
,
which
blow
almost
continually
during
the
greatest
part
of
the
year
;
but
it
is
also
subject
to
putrid
vapours
,
arising
from
the
low
marshy
ground
in
the
neighbourhood
of
the
harbour
,
which
is
every
tide
overflowed
with
seawater
.
<EOS>
This
may
be
one
cause
of
the
scrofula
and
rickets
,
which
are
two
prevailing
disorders
among
the
children
in
Boulogne
.
<EOS>
But
I
believe
the
former
is
more
owing
to
the
water
used
in
the
Lower
Town
,
which
is
very
hard
and
unwholsome
.
<EOS>
It
curdles
with
soap
,
gives
a
red
colour
to
the
meat
that
is
boiled
in
it
,
and
,
when
drank
by
strangers
,
never
fails
to
occasion
pains
in
the
stomach
and
bowels
;
nay
,
sometimes
produces
dysenteries
.
<EOS>
In
all
appearance
it
is
impregnated
with
nitre
,
if
not
with
something
more
mischievous
:
we
know
that
mundic
,
or
pyrites
,
very
often
contains
a
proportion
of
arsenic
,
mixed
with
sulphur
,
vitriol
,
and
mercury
.
<EOS>
Perhaps
it
partakes
of
the
acid
of
some
coal
mine
;
for
there
are
coal
works
in
this
district
.
<EOS>
There
is
a
well
of
purging
water
within
a
quarter
of
a
mile
of
the
Upper
Town
,
to
which
the
inhabitants
resort
in
the
morning
,
as
the
people
of
London
go
to
the
Dog
-
and
-
duck
,
in
St
George
's
fields
.
<EOS>
There
is
likewise
a
fountain
of
excellent
water
,
hard
by
the
cathedral
,
in
the
Upper
Town
,
from
whence
I
am
daily
supplied
at
a
small
expence
.
<EOS>
Some
modern
chemists
affirm
,
that
no
saline
chalybeate
waters
can
exist
,
except
in
the
neighbourhood
of
coal
damps
;
and
that
nothing
can
be
more
mild
,
and
gentle
,
and
friendly
to
the
constitution
,
than
the
said
damps
:
<EOS>
but
I
know
that
the
place
where
I
was
bred
stands
upon
a
zonic
of
coal
;
that
the
water
which
the
inhabitants
generally
use
is
hard
and
brackish
;
and
that
the
people
are
remarkably
subject
to
the
king
's
evil
and
consumption
.
<EOS>
These
I
would
impute
to
the
bad
water
,
impregnated
with
the
vitriol
and
brine
of
coal
,
as
there
is
nothing
in
the
constitution
of
the
air
that
should
render
such
distempers
endemial
.
<EOS>
That
the
air
of
Boulogne
encourages
putrefaction
,
appears
from
the
effect
it
has
upon
butcher
's
meat
,
which
,
though
the
season
is
remarkably
cold
,
we
can
hardly
keep
four
-
and
-
twenty
hours
in
the
coolest
part
of
the
house
.
  
<EOS>
Living
here
is
pretty
reasonable
;
and
the
markets
are
tolerably
supplied
.
<EOS>
The
beef
is
neither
fat
nor
firm
;
but
very
good
for
soup
,
which
is
the
only
use
the
French
make
of
it
.
<EOS>
The
veal
is
not
so
white
,
nor
so
well
fed
,
as
the
English
veal
;
but
it
is
more
juicy
,
and
better
tasted
.
<EOS>
The
mutton
and
pork
are
very
good
.
<EOS>
We
buy
our
poultry
alive
,
and
fatten
them
at
home
.
<EOS>
Here
are
excellent
turkies
,
and
no
want
of
game
:
the
hares
,
in
particular
,
are
very
large
,
juicy
,
and
high
-
flavoured
.
<EOS>
The
best
part
of
the
fish
caught
on
this
coast
is
sent
post
to
Paris
,
in
chasse
-
marines
,
by
a
company
of
contractors
,
like
those
of
Hastings
in
Sussex
.
<EOS>
Nevertheless
,
we
have
excellent
soles
,
skaite
,
flounders
and
whitings
,
and
sometimes
mackarel
.
<EOS>
The
oysters
are
very
large
,
coarse
,
and
rank
.
<EOS>
There
is
very
little
fish
caught
on
the
French
coast
,
because
the
shallows
run
a
great
way
from
the
shore
;
and
the
fish
live
chiefly
in
deep
water
:
for
this
reason
the
fishermen
go
a
great
way
out
to
sea
,
sometimes
even
as
far
as
the
coast
of
England
.
<EOS>
Notwithstanding
all
the
haste
the
contractors
can
make
,
their
fish
in
the
summer
is
very
often
spoiled
before
it
arrives
at
Paris
;
and
this
is
not
to
be
wondered
at
,
considering
the
length
of
the
way
,
which
is
near
one
hundred
and
fifty

.
<EOS>
At
best

must
be
in
such
a

,
that
no
other
people
,
except
the
negroes
on
the
coast
of

,
would
feed
upon

.
  
<EOS>
The
wine
commonly

at
Boulogne
comes
from
Auxerre
,
is
very
small
and
meagre
,
and
may
be
had
from
five
to
eight
sols
a
bottle
;
that
is
,
from
two
-
pence
halfpenny
to
fourpence
.
<EOS>
The
French
inhabitants
drink
no
good
wine
;
nor
is
there
any
to
be
had
,
unless
you
have
recourse
to
the
British

-

here
established
,
who
deal
in
Bourdeaux

,
brought
hither
by
sea
for
the

market
.
<EOS>
I
have
very
good
claret
from
a
friend
,
at
the
rate
of
fifteen
-
pence
sterling
a
bottle
;
and
excellent
small
beer
as
reasonable
as
in
England
.
<EOS>
I
do
n't
believe
there
is
a
drop
of
generous
Burgundy
in
the
place
;
and
the
aubergistes
impose
upon

,
when
they

at
two
livres

.
<EOS>
There
is
a
small
white
wine
,
called
preniac
,
which
is
very
agreeable
and
very
cheap
.
<EOS>
All

brandy
which
I
have
seen
in
Boulogne
is
new
,
fiery
,
and
still
-

.
<EOS>
This
is
the
trash
which
the
smugglers
import
into
England
:
they
have
it
for
about
ten
-
pence
a
gallon
.
<EOS>
Butcher
's
meat
is
sold
for
five
sols
,
or
two
-
pence
halfpenny
a
pound
,
and
the
pound
here
consists
of
eighteen
ounces
.
<EOS>
I
have
a
young
turkey
for
thirty
sols
;
a
hare
for
four
-
and
-
twenty
;
a
couple
of
chickens
for
twenty
sols
,
and
a
couple
of
good
soles
for
the
same

.
<EOS>
Before
we
left
England
,
we
were

that

no

in
Boulogne
;
but
we
have
found
ourselves
agreeably

this

.
<EOS>
The
place
is
well
supplied
with
strawberries
,
cherries
,
gooseberries
,
corinths
,
peaches
,
apricots
,
and

.
<EOS>
I
have
eaten
more
fruit
this
season
,
than
I
have
done
for
several
years
.
<EOS>
There
are
many
well
-
cultivated
gardens
in
the
skirts
of
the
town
;
particularly
one
belonging
to
our
friend
Mrs
B
 
,
where
we
often
drink
tea
in
a
charming
summer
-
house
built
on
a
rising
ground
,
which
commands
a
delightful

of
the
sea
.
<EOS>
We
have
many

to
this
good
lady
,
who
is
a
kind
neighbour
,
an
obliging
friend
,
and
a
most

:
she
speaks
English

,
and
is
greatly
attached
to
the
people
and
the

of
our

.
<EOS>
They
use
wood
for
their
common
fewel
,
though
,
if
I
were
to
live
at
Boulogne
,
I
would
mix

with
coal
,
which
this

.
<EOS>
Both
the
wood
and
the
coal
are
reasonable
enough
.
<EOS>
I
am
certain
that
a
man
may
keep
house
in
Boulogne
for
about
one
half
of
what
it
will
cost
him
in
London
;
and
this
is
said
to
be
one

the
dearest

France
.
  
<EOS>
The
adjacent
country
is
very
agreeable
,
diversified
with
hill
and
dale
,
corn
-
fields
,
woods
,
and
meadows
.
<EOS>
There
is
a
forest
of
a
considerable

,
that

about

from
the
Upper
Town
:
it
belongs
to
the
king
,
and
the
wood
is
farmed
to
different

.
  
<EOS>
In
point
of
agriculture
,
the
people
in
this
neighbourhood
seem
to
have
profited
by
the
example
of
the
English
.
<EOS>
Since
I
was
last
in
France
,
fifteen

ago
,
a
good
number
of
inclosures
and
plantations
have
been
made
in
the
English

.
<EOS>
There
is
a
good
many
tolerable
country
-
houses
,
within
a
few

of
Boulogne
;
but
mostly
empty
.
<EOS>
I
was
offered
a
compleat
house
,
with
a
garden
of
four

well
laid

,
and
two
fields
for
grass
or

,
about
a

from
the
town
,
for
four
hundred

,
about
seventeen

a

:
it
is
partly
furnished
,
stands
in
an
agreeable

,
with
a
fine
prospect
of
the
sea
,
and
was
lately
occupied
by
a
Scotch
nobleman
,
who
is
in
the
service
of
France
.
  
<EOS>
To
judge
from
appearance
,
the
people
of
Boulogne
are
descended
from
the
Flemings
,
who
formerly
possessed
this

;
for
,
a
great
many

the
present
inhabitants
have
fine

,

,

;
very

the
natives
of
France
in

,
who
are
distinguished
by
black

,
brown

,

.
<EOS>
The
people
of
the
Boulonnois
enjoy
some

,
and
,
in

,
are
exempted
from
the
gabelle
or
duties
upon
salt
:
<EOS>
how

deserved
this

favour
,
I
do
not

;
but
they
seem
to
have
a
spirit

among
them
,
are
very

,
and
much

.
<EOS>
Many

,
both

the
town
and

;
and
the
peasants
,
from

,

'

.
<EOS>
Several

of
this

have

in
the
course

the
last

.
<EOS>
The

is
given
,
in

,
to
the

of
justice
,
by
the

of
the
great
,
has
always

.
<EOS>
The
peasants
too
are
often
rendered

,
by
the

from
the
oppression
and

.
<EOS>
In
this
neighbourhood
the
labouring
people
are
ill
lodged
and
wretchedly
fed
;
and
they
have
no
idea
of
cleanliness
.
<EOS>
There
is
a
substantial
burgher
in
the
High
Town
,
who
was
some

ago

of
a
most

.
<EOS>
He
received

to

broke
alive
upon

;
but
was
pardoned
by
the
interposition
of
the
governor
of
the
county
,
and
carries
on

as
usual
in
the
face
of
the
whole

.
<EOS>
A
furious
abbe
,
being

by

bishop
,
on

,

with

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

bishop
desired
he
might

;
but
it
was
thought

to
punish
,
with

,

.
<EOS>
He

,
and
,

,

to

.
<EOS>
When

,

,
that

was

should
undergo

,

,

,
while
such
-
a
-

(
naming

)

,
after

a

,
and
a
helpless

big
with

,
who
had
not

.
  
<EOS>
The
inhabitants
of
Boulogne
may
be
divided
into
three
classes
;
the
noblesse
or
gentry
,
the
burghers
,
and
the
canaille
.
<EOS>
I
do
n't
mention
the
clergy
,
and
the
people
belonging
to
the
law
,
because
I
shall
occasionally
trouble

with
my

upon
the
religion
and

of
this
country
;
and
as

the

,
exclusive

their

,
they
may

considered

belonging
to
one

other

these

.
<EOS>
The
noblesse
are
vain
,
proud
,
poor
,
and
slothful
.
<EOS>
Very
few
of
them
have
above
six
thousand
livres
a
year
,
which
may
amount
to
about
two
hundred
and

pounds
sterling
;
and
many
of
them
have
not
half

.
<EOS>
I
think
there
is
one
heiress
,
said
to
be
worth
one
hundred
thousand
livres
,
about
four
thousand
two
hundred
pounds
;
but
then
her
jewels
,
her
cloaths
,
and
even
her
linen
,
are
reckoned

of
this
fortune
.
<EOS>
The
noblesse
have
not
the
common
sense
to
reside
at
their
houses
in
the
country
,
where
,
by
farming
their
own
grounds
,
they
might
live
at
a
small

,
and
improve

.
<EOS>
They
allow
their
country
houses
to
go
to
decay
,
and
their

and
fields
to
waste
;
and
reside
in
dark
holes
in
the
Upper
Town
of
Boulogne
without
light
,
air
,
or
convenience
.
<EOS>
There
they
starve
within
doors
,
that
they
may
have
wherewithal
to
purchase
fine
cloaths
,
and
appear

once
a
day
in
the
church
,
or

the

.
<EOS>
They
have
no
education
,
no
taste
for
reading
,
no

,
nor
indeed
any
earthly
occupation
,
but
that
of
dressing
their
hair
,
and
adorning
their
bodies
.
<EOS>
They
hate
walking
,
and
would
never
go
abroad
,
if
they
were
not
stimulated
by
the
vanity
of
being
seen
.
<EOS>
I
ought
to
except
indeed
those
who
turn
devotees
,
and
spend
the
greatest
part
of
their
time
with
the
priest
,
either
at
church
or
in
their
own
houses
.
<EOS>
Other
amusements
they
have
none
in
this
place
,
except
private
parties
of
card
-
playing
,
which
are
far
from
being
expensive
.
<EOS>
Nothing
can
be
more
parsimonious
than
the
oeconomy
of
these
people
:
they
live
upon
soupe
and
bouille
,
fish
and
sallad
:
they
never
think
of
giving
dinners
,
or
entertaining
their
friends
;
they
even
save
the
expence
of
coffee
and
tea
,
though
both
are
very
cheap
at
Boulogne
.
<EOS>
They
presume
that
every
person
drinks
coffee
at
home
,
immediately

dinner
,
which
is
always
over
by
one
o'clock
;
and
,
in
lieu
of
tea
in
the
afternoon
,
they
treat
with
a
glass
of
sherbet
,
or

.
<EOS>
In
a
word
,
I
know
not
a
more

set

than
the
noblesse
of
Boulogne
;
helpless
in
themselves
,
and
useless
to
the
community
;
without
dignity
,
sense
,
or
sentiment
;
contemptible
from
pride
.
<EOS>
and
ridiculous
from
vanity
.
<EOS>
They
pretend
to
be
jealous
of
their
rank
,
and
will
entertain
no
correspondence
with
the
merchants
,
whom

.
<EOS>
They
likewise
keep
at
a
great

from

,
on

a

in
the
article

:
but
,

informed
,
this

is
in

,
in

to

poverty
,
which
would
appear

greater

,
if

a

.
<EOS>
Considering
the
vivacity
of
the
French

,

not

lead

,

,

.
<EOS>
True

,

of
this

a
puppet

and

;
but

their

.
<EOS>
Their
high

,
their

,
their

,
their

,
confessions
,
images
,
tapers
,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

to

.
<EOS>
If

,

.
<EOS>
The

are
so

being

this

,
that

amuses

in
the
most

,
and
keeps

.
<EOS>
A
Roman

longs

,

,

,

;
and

.
<EOS>
Even

,

,

ought

,

,

:

,

,

;
and
ten
to
one
<EOS>
but

.
<EOS>
And

,

,

the

,

,

.
<EOS>
Veronique
,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

St
Suaire
,

.
<EOS>
This
same
handkerchief
is
said

have

three
folds
,
on

which

the
impression
:
one

,

,

.
<EOS>
Baronius

,
there

.
<EOS>
Tillemont
,
however
,
looks
upon
the
whole
as
a
fable
.
<EOS>
Some
suppose
Veronica
to
be
the
same
with
St
Haemorrhoissa
,
the
patroness
of
those
who
are
afflicted
with
the
piles
,
who
make
their
joint
invocations
to
her
and
St
Fiacre
,
the
son
of
a
Scotch
king
,
who
lived
and
died
a
hermit
in
France
.
<EOS>
The
troops
of
Henry
V
of
England
are
said
to
have
pillaged
the
chapel
of
this
Highland
saint
;
who
,
in
revenge
,
assisted
his
countrymen
,
in
the
French
service
,
to
defeat
the
English
at
Bauge
,
and
afterwards
afflicted
Henry
with
the
piles
,
of
which
he
died
.
<EOS>
This
prince
complained
,
that
he
was
not
only
plagued
by
the
living
Scots
,
but
even
persecuted
by
those
who
were
dead
.
  
<EOS>
I
know
not
whether
I
may
be
allowed
to
compare
the
Romish
religion
to
comedy
,
and
Calvinism
to
tragedy
.
<EOS>
The
first
amuses
the
senses
,
and
excites
ideas
of
mirth
and
good
-
humour
;
the
other
,
like
tragedy
,
deals
in
the
passions
of
terror
and
pity
.
<EOS>
Step
into
a
conventicle
of
dissenters
,
you
will
,
ten
to
one
,
hear
the
minister
holding
forth
upon
the
sufferings
of
Christ
,
or
the
torments
of
hell
,
and
see
many
marks
of
religious
horror
in
the
faces
of
the
hearers
.
<EOS>
This
is
perhaps
one
reason
why
the
reformation
did
not
succeed
in
France
,
among
a
volatile
,
giddy
,
unthinking
people
,
shocked
at
the
mortified
appearances
of
the
Calvinists
;
and
accounts
for
its
rapid
progress
among
nations
of
a
more
melancholy
turn
of
character
and
complexion
:
for
,
in
the
conversion
of
the
multitude
,
reason
is
generally
out
of
the
question
.
<EOS>
Even
the
penance
imposed
upon
the
catholics
is
little
more
than
mock
mortification
:
a
murderer
is
often
quit
with
his
confessor
for
saying
three
prayers
extraordinary
;
and
these
easy
terms
,
on
which
absolution
is
obtained
,
certainly
encourage
the
repetition
of
the
most
enormous
crimes
.
<EOS>
The
pomp
and
ceremonies
of
this
religion
,
together
with
the
great
number
of
holidays
they
observe
,
howsoever
<EOS>
they
may
keep
up
the
spirits
of
the
commonalty
,
and
help
to
diminish
the
sense
of
their
own
misery
,
must
certainly
,
at
the
same
time
,
produce
a
frivolous
taste
for
frippery
and
shew
,
and
encourage
a
habit
of
idleness
,
to
which
I
,
in
a
great
measure
,
ascribe
the
extreme
poverty
of
the
lower
people
.
<EOS>
Very
near
half
of
their
time
,
which
might
he
profitably
employed
in
the
exercise
of
industry
,
is
lost
to
themselves
and
the
community
,
in
attendance
upon
the
different
exhibitions
of
religious
mummery
.
  
<EOS>
But
as
this
letter
has
already
run
to
an
unconscionable
length
,
I
shall
defer
,
till
another
occasion
,
what
I
have
further
to
say
on
the
people
of
this
place
,
and
in
the
mean
time
assure
you
,
that
I
am
always
 
Yours
affectionately
.
   
<EOS>
BOULOGNE
,
September
12
,
1763
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
My
stay
in
this
place
now
draws
towards
a
period
.
 
<EOS>
'
Till
within
these
few
days
I
have
continued
bathing
,
with
some
advantage
to
my
health
,
though
the
season
has
been
cold
and
wet
,
and
disagreeable
.
<EOS>
There
was
a
fine
prospect
of
a
plentiful
harvest
in
this
neighbourhood
.
<EOS>
I
used
to
have
great
pleasure
in
driving
between
the
fields
of
wheat
,
oats
,
and
barley
;
but
the
crop
has
been
entirely
ruined
by
the
rain
,
and
nothing
is
now
to
be
seen
on
the
ground
but
the
tarnished
straw
,
and
the
rotten
spoils
of
the
husbandman
's
labour
.
<EOS>
The
ground
scarce
affords
subsistence
to
a
few
flocks
of
meagre
sheep
,
that
crop
the
stubble
,
and
the
intervening
grass
;
each
flock
under
the
protection
of
its
shepherd
,
with
his
crook
and
dogs
,
who
lies
every
night
in
the
midst
of
the
fold
,
in
a
little
thatched
travelling
lodge
,
mounted
on
a
wheel
-
carriage
.
<EOS>
Here
he
passes
the
night
,
in
order
to
defend
his
flock
from
the
wolves
,
which
are
sometimes
,
especially
in
winter
,
very
bold
and
desperate
.
  
<EOS>
Two
days
ago
we
made
an
excursion
with
Mrs
B
  
and
Capt
L
  
to
the
village
of
Samers
,
on
the
Paris
road
,
about
three
leagues
from
Boulogne
.
<EOS>
Here
is
a
venerable
abbey
of
Benedictines
,
well
endowed
,
with
large
agreeable
gardens
prettily
laid
out
.
<EOS>
The
monks
are
well
lodged
,
and
well
entertained
.
<EOS>
Tho
'
restricted
from
flesh
meals
by
the
rules
of
their
order
,
they
are
allowed
to
eat
wild
duck
and
teal
,
as
a
species
of
fish
;
and
when
they
long
for
a
good
bouillon
,
or
a
partridge
,
or
pullet
,
they
have
nothing
to
do
but
to
say
they
are
out
of
order
.
<EOS>
In
that
case
the
appetite
of
the
patient
is
indulged
in
his
own
apartment
.
<EOS>
Their
church
is
elegantly
contrived
,
but
kept
in
a
very
dirty
condition
.
<EOS>
The
greatest
curiosity
I
saw
in
this
place
was
an
English
boy
,
about
eight
or
nine
years
old
,
whom
his
father
had
sent
hither
to
learn
the
French
language
.
<EOS>
In
less
than
eight
weeks
,
he
was
become
captain
of
the
boys
of
the
place
,
spoke
French
perfectly
well
,
and
had
almost
forgot
his
mother
tongue
.
<EOS>
But
to
return
to
the
people
of
Boulogne
.
  
<EOS>
The
burghers
here
,
as
in
other
places
,
consist
of
merchants
,
shop
-
keepers
,
and
artisans
.
<EOS>
Some
of
the
merchants
have
got
fortunes
,
by
fitting
out
privateers
during
the
war
.
<EOS>
A
great
many
single
ships
were
taken
from
the
English
,
notwithstanding
the
good
look
-
out
of
our
cruisers
,
who
were
so
alert
,
that
the
privateers
from
this
coast
were
often
taken
in
four
hours
after
they
sailed
from
the
French
harbour
;
and
there
is
hardly
a
captain
of
an
armateur
in
Boulogne
,
who
has
not
been
prisoner
in
England
five
or
six
times
in
the
course
of
the
war
.
<EOS>
They
were
fitted
out
at
a
very
small
expence
,
and
used
to
run
over
in
the
night
to
the
coast
of
England
,
where
they
hovered
as
English
fishing
smacks
,
until
they
kidnapped
some
coaster
,
with
which
they
made
the
best
of
their
way
across
the
Channel
.
<EOS>
If
they
fell
in
with
a
British
cruiser
,
they
surrendered
without
resistance
:
the
captain
was
soon
exchanged
,
and
the
loss
of
the
proprietor
was
not
great
:
if
they
brought
their
prize
safe
into
harbour
,
the
advantage
was
considerable
.
<EOS>
In
time
of
peace
the
merchants
of
Boulogne
deal
in
wine
brandies
,
and
oil
,
imported
from
the
South
,
and
export
fish
,
with
the
manufactures
of
France
,
to
Portugal
,
and
other
countries
;
but
the
trade
is
not
great
.
<EOS>
Here
are
two
or
three
considerable
houses
of
wine
merchants
from
Britain
,
who
deal
in
Bourdeaux
wine
,
with
which
they
supply
London
and
other
parts
of
England
,
Scotland
,
and
Ireland
.
<EOS>
The
fishery
of
mackarel
and
herring
is
so
considerable
on
this
coast
,
that
it
is
said
to
yield
annually
eight
or
nine
hundred
thousand
livres
,
about
thirty
-
five
thousand
pounds
sterling
.
  
<EOS>
The
shop
-
keepers
here
drive
a
considerable
traffic
with
the
English
smugglers
,
whose
cutters
are
almost
the
only
vessels
one
sees
in
the
harbour
of
Boulogne
,
if
we
except
about
a
dozen
of
those
flat
-
bottomed
boats
,
which
raised
such
alarms
in
England
,
in
the
course
of
the
war
.
<EOS>
Indeed
they
seem
to
be
good
for
nothing
else
,
and
perhaps
they
were
built
for
this
purpose
only
.
<EOS>
The
smugglers
from
the
coast
of
Kent
and
Sussex
pay
English
gold
for
great
quantities
of
French
brandy
,
tea
,
coffee
,
and
small
wine
,
which
they
run
from
this
country
.
<EOS>
They
likewise
buy
glass
trinkets
,
toys
,
and
coloured
prints
,
which
sell
in
England
,
for
no
other
reason
,
but
that
they
come
from
France
,
as
they
may
be
had
as
cheap
,
and
much
better
finished
,
of
our
own
manufacture
.
<EOS>
They
likewise
take
off
ribbons
,
laces
,
linen
,
and
cambrics
;
though
this
branch
of
trade
is
chiefly
in
the
hands
of
traders
that
come
from
London
and
make
their
purchases
at
Dunkirk
,
where
they
pay
no
duties
.
<EOS>
It
is
certainly
worth
while
for
any
traveller
to
lay
in
a
stock
of
linen
either
at
Dunkirk
or
Boulogne
;
the
difference
of
the
price
at
these
two
places
is
not
great
.
<EOS>
Even
here
I
have
made
a
provision
of
shirts
for
one
half
of
the
money
they
would
have
cost
in
London
.
<EOS>
Undoubtedly
the
practice
of
smuggling
is
very
detrimental
to
the
fair
trader
,
and
carries
considerable
sums
of
money
out
of
the
kingdom
,
to
enrich
our
rivals
and
enemies
.
<EOS>
The
custom
-
house
officers
are
very
watchful
,
and
make
a
great
number
of
seizures
:
nevertheless
,
the
smugglers
find
their
account
in
continuing
this
contraband
commerce
;
and
are
said
to
indemnify
themselves
,
if
they
save
one
cargo
out
of
three
.
<EOS>
After
all
,
the
best
way
to
prevent
smuggling
,
is
to
lower
the
duties
upon
the
commodities
which
are
thus
introduced
.
<EOS>
I
have
been
told
,
that
the
revenue
upon
tea
has
encreased
ever
since
the
duty
upon
it
was
diminished
.
<EOS>
By
the
bye
,
the
tea
smuggled
on
the
coast
of
Sussex
is
most
execrable
stuff
.
<EOS>
While
I
stayed
at
Hastings
,
for
the
conveniency
of
bathing
,
I
must
have
changed
my
breakfast
,
if
I
had
not
luckily
brought
tea
with
me
from
London
:
yet
we
have
as
good
tea
at
Boulogne
for
nine
livres
a
pound
,
as
that
which
sells
at
fourteen
shillings
at
London
.
  
<EOS>
The
bourgeois
of
this
place
seem
to
live
at
their
ease
,
probably
in
consequence
of
their
trade
with
the
English
.
<EOS>
Their
houses
consist
of
the
ground
-
floor
,
one
story
above
,
and
garrets
.
<EOS>
In
those
which
are
well
furnished
,
you
see
pier
-
glasses
and
marble
slabs
;
but
the
chairs
are
either
paultry
things
,
made
with
straw
bottoms
,
which
cost
about
a
shilling
a
-
piece
,
or
old
-
fashioned
,
high
-
backed
seats
of
needle
-
work
,
stuffed
,
very
clumsy
and
incommodious
.
<EOS>
The
tables
are
square
fir
boards
,
that
stand
on
edge
in
a
corner
,
except
when
they
are
used
,
and
then
they
are
set
upon
cross
legs
that
open
and
shut
occasionally
.
<EOS>
The
king
of
France
dines
off
a
board
of
this
kind
.
<EOS>
Here
is
plenty
of
table
-
linen
however
.
<EOS>
The
poorest
tradesman
in
Boulogne
has
a
napkin
on
every
cover
,
and
silver
forks
with
four
prongs
,
which
are
used
with
the
right
hand
,
there
being
very
little
occasion
for
knives
;
for
the
meat
is
boiled
or
roasted
to
rags
.
<EOS>
The
French
beds
are
so
high
,
that
sometimes
one
is
obliged
to
mount
them
by
the
help
of
steps
;
and
this
is
also
the
case
in
Flanders
.
<EOS>
They
very
seldom
use
feather
-
beds
;
but
they
lie
upon
a
paillasse
,
or
bag
of
straw
,
over
which
are
laid
two
,
and
sometimes
three
mattrasses
.
<EOS>
Their
testers
are
high
and
old
-
fashioned
,
and
their
curtains
generally
of
thin
bays
,
red
,
or
green
,
laced
with
taudry
yellow
,
in
imitation
of
gold
.
<EOS>
In
some
houses
,
however
,
one
meets
with
furniture
of
stamped
linen
;
but
there
is
no
such
thing
as
a
carpet
to
be
seen
,
and
the
floors
are
in
a
very
dirty
condition
.
<EOS>
They
have
not
even
the
implements
of
cleanliness
in
this
country
.
<EOS>
Every
chamber
is
furnished
with
an
armoire
,
or
clothes
-
press
,
and
a
chest
of
drawers
,
of
very
clumsy
workmanship
.
<EOS>
Every
thing
shews
a
deficiency
in
the
mechanic
arts
.
<EOS>
There
is
not
a
door
,
nor
a
window
,
that
shuts
close
.
<EOS>
The
hinges
,
locks
,
and
latches
,
are
of
iron
,
coarsely
made
,
and
ill
contrived
.
<EOS>
The
very
chimnies
are
built
so
open
,
that
they
admit
both
rain
and
sun
,
and
all
of
them
smoke
intolerably
.
<EOS>
If
there
is
no
cleanliness
among
these
people
,
much
less
shall
we
find
delicacy
,
which
is
the
cleanliness
of
the
mind
.
<EOS>
Indeed
they
are
utter
strangers
to
what
we
call
common
decency
;
and
I
could
give
you
some
high
-
flavoured
instances
,
at
which
even
a
native
of
Edinburgh
would
stop
his
nose
.
<EOS>
There
are
certain
mortifying
views
of
human
nature
,
which
undoubtedly
ought
to
be
concealed
as
much
as
possible
,
in
order
to
prevent
giving
offence
:
and
nothing
can
be
more
absurd
,
than
to
plead
the
difference
of
custom
in
different
countries
,
in
defence
of
these
usages
which
can
not
fail
giving
disgust
to
the
organs
and
senses
of
all
mankind
.
<EOS>
Will
custom
exempt
from
the
imputation
of
gross
indecency
a
French
lady
,
who
shifts
her
frowsy
smock
in
presence
of
a
male
visitant
,
and
talks
to
him
of
her
lavement
,
her
medecine
,
and
her
bidet
!
<EOS>
An
Italian
signora
makes
no
scruple
of
telling
you
,
she
is
such
a
day
to
begin
a
course
of
physic
for
the
pox
.
<EOS>
The
celebrated
reformer
of
the
Italian
comedy
introduces
a
child
befouling
itself
,
on
the
stage
,
OE
,
NO
TI
SENTI
?

side
occasionally
,
according
as
the
wind
blows
,
so
that
sometimes
the
right
and
sometimes
the
left
hand
guides
the
beast
:
but
in
other
parts
of
France
,
as
well
as
in
Italy
,
the
ladies
sit
on
horseback
with
their
legs
astride
,
and
are
provided
with
drawers
for
that
purpose
.
  
<EOS>
When
I
said
the
French
people
were
kept
in
good
humour
by
the
fopperies
of
their
religion
,
I
did
not
mean
that
there
were
no
gloomy
spirits
among
them
.
<EOS>
There
will
be
fanatics
in
religion
,
while
there
are
people
of
a
saturnine
disposition
,
and
melancholy
turn
of
mind
.
<EOS>
The
character
of
a
devotee
,
which
is
hardly
known
in
England
,
is
very
common
here
.
<EOS>
You
see
them
walking
to
and
from
church
at
all
hours
,
in
their
hoods
and
long
camblet
cloaks
,
with
a
slow
pace
,
demure
aspect
,
and
downcast
eye
.
<EOS>
Those
who
are
poor
become
very
troublesome
to
the
monks
,
with
their
scruples
and
cases
of
conscience
:
you
may
see
them
on
their
knees
,
at
the
confessional
,
every
hour
in
the
day
.
<EOS>
The
rich
devotee
has
her
favourite
confessor
,
whom
she
consults
and
regales
in
private
,
at
her
own
house
;
and
this
spiritual
director
generally
governs
the
whole
family
.
<EOS>
For
my
part
I
never
knew
a
fanatic
that
was
not
an
hypocrite
at
bottom
.
<EOS>
Their
pretensions
to
superior
sanctity
,
and
an
absolute
conquest
over
all
the
passions
,
which
human
reason
was
never
yet
able
to
subdue
,
introduce
a
habit
of
dissimulation
,
which
,
like
all
other
habits
,
is
confirmed
by
use
,
till
at
length
they
become
adepts
in
the
art
and
science
of
hypocrisy
.
<EOS>
Enthusiasm
and
hypocrisy
are
by
no
means
incompatible
.
<EOS>
The
wildest
fanatics
I
ever
knew
,
were
real
sensualists
in
their
way
of
living
,
and
cunning
cheats
in
their
dealings
with
mankind
.
  
<EOS>
Among
the
lower
class
of
people
at
Boulogne
,
those
who
take
the
lead
,
are
the
sea
-
faring
men
,
who
live
in
one
quarter
,
divided
into
classes
,
and
registered
for
the
service
of
the
king
.
<EOS>
They
are
hardy
and
raw
-
boned
,
exercise
the
trade
of
fishermen
and
boatmen
,
and
propagate
like
rabbits
.
<EOS>
They
have
put
themselves
under
the
protection
of
a
miraculous
image
of
the
Virgin
Mary
,
which
is
kept
in
one
of
their
churches
,
and
every
year
carried
in
procession
.
<EOS>
According
to
the
legend
,
this
image
was
carried
off
,
with
other
pillage
,
by
the
English
,
when
they
took
Boulogne
,
in
the
reign
of
Henry
VIII
.
<EOS>
The
lady
,
rather
than
reside
in
England
,
where
she
found
a
great
many
heretics
,
trusted
herself
alone
in
an
open
boat
,
and
crossed
the
sea
to
the
road
of
Boulogne
,
where
she
was
seen
waiting
for
a
pilot
.
<EOS>
Accordingly
a
boat
put
off
to
her
assistance
,
and
brought
her
safe
into
the
harbour
:
since
which
time
she
has
continued
to
patronize
the
watermen
of
Boulogne
.
<EOS>
At
present
she
is
very
black
and
very
ugly
,
besides
being
cruelly
mutilated
in
different
parts
of
her
body
,
which
I

have
been
amputated
,
and
converted
into
tobacco
-
stoppers
;
but
once
a
year
she
is
dressed
in
very
rich
attire
,
and
carried
in
procession
,
with
a
silver
boat
,
provided
at
the
expence
of
the
sailors
.
<EOS>
That
vanity
which
characterises
the
French
extends
even
to
the
canaille
.
<EOS>
The
lowest
creature
among
them
is
sure
to
have
her
ear
-
rings
and
golden
cross
hanging
about
her
neck
.
<EOS>
Indeed
this
last
is
an
implement
of
superstition
as
well
as
of
dress
,
without
which
no
female
appears
.
<EOS>
The
common
people
here
,
as
in
all
countries
where
they
live
poorly
and
dirtily
,
are
hard
-
featured
,
and
of
very
brown
,
or
rather

.
<EOS>
As
they
seldom
eat
meat
,
their
juices
are
destitute
of
that
animal
oil
which
gives
a
plumpness
and
smoothness
to
the
skin
,
and
defends
those
fine
capillaries
from
the
injuries
of
the
weather
,
which
would
otherwise
coalesce
,
or
be
shrunk
up
,
so
as
to
impede
the
circulation
on
the
external
surface
of
the
body
.
<EOS>
As
for
the
dirt
,
it
undoubtedly
blocks
up
the
pores
of
the
skin
,
and
disorders
the
perspiration
;
consequently
must
contribute
to
the
scurvy
,
itch
,
and
other
cutaneous
distempers
.
  
<EOS>
In
the
quarter
of
the
matelots
at
Boulogne
,
there
is
a
number
of
poor
Canadians
,
who
were
removed
from
the
island
of
St
John
,
in
the
gulph
of
St
Laurence
,
when
it
was
reduced
by
the
English
.
<EOS>
These
people
are
maintained
at
the
expence
of
the
king
,
who
allows
them
soldier
's
pay
,
that
is
five
sols
,
or
two
-
pence
halfpenny
a
day
;
or
rather
three
sols
and
ammunition
bread
.
<EOS>
How
the
soldiers
contrive
to
subsist
upon
this
wretched
allowance
,
I
can
not
comprehend
:
<EOS>
but
,
it
must
be
owned
,
<EOS>
that
those
invalids
who
do
duty
at
Boulogne
betray
no
marks
of
want
.
<EOS>
They
are
hale
and
stout
,
neatly
and
decently
cloathed
,
and
on
the
whole
look
better
than
the
pensioners
of
Chelsea
.
  
<EOS>
About
three
weeks
ago
I
was
favoured
with
a
visit
by
one
<EOS>
Mr
M
 
,
an
English
gentleman
,
who
seems
far
gone
in
a
consumption
.
<EOS>
He
passed
the
last
winter
at
Nismes
in
Languedoc
,
and
found
himself
much
better
in
the
beginning
of
summer
,
when
he
embarked
at
Cette
,
and
returned
by
sea

England
.
<EOS>
He
soon
relapsed
,
however
,
and
(
as
he
imagines
)
in
consequence
of
a
cold
caught
at
sea
.
<EOS>
He
told

,
his
intention

to
try
the
South
again
,
and
even
to
go
as

as
Italy
.
<EOS>
I
advised

to
make
trial
of
the
air
of
Nice
,
where
I
myself
proposed
to
reside
.
<EOS>
He
seemed
to
relish
my
advice
,
and
proceeded
towards
Paris
in
his
own
carriage
.
  
<EOS>
I
shall
to
-
morrow
ship
<EOS>
my
great
chests
on
board
of
a
ship
bound
to
Bourdeaux
;
they
are
directed
,
and
recommended

the
care
of
a

of
that
place
,
who
will
forward

by
Thoulouse
,
and
the
canal
of
Languedoc
,
to
his

at
Cette
,
which
is
the
sea
-
port
of
Montpellier
.
<EOS>
The

of
their

to
Bourdeaux
does
not

one

.
<EOS>
They
consist
of
two
very
large
chests
and
a
trunk
,
about

weight
;
and
the
expence
of
transporting

from
Bourdeaux
to
Cette
,
will
not

thirty

.
<EOS>
They
are
already
sealed
with
lead
at
the
customhouse
,
that
they
may
be
exempted
from
further
visitation
.
<EOS>
This
is
a
precaution
which
every
traveller
takes
,
both
by
sea
and
land
:
he
must
likewise
provide
himself
with
a
passe
-
avant
at
the
bureau
,
otherwise
he
may
be
stopped
,
and
rummaged
at
every
town
through
which
he
passes
.
<EOS>
I
have
hired
a
berline
and
four
horses
to
Paris
,
for
fourteen
loui'dores
;
two
of
which
the
voiturier
is
obliged
to
pay
for
a
permission
from
the
farmers
of
the
poste
;
for
every
thing
is
farmed
in
this
country
;
and
if

hire

carriage
,
as

have

,
you
must
pay
twelve
livres
,
or
half
-
a
-
guinea
,
for
every

in

.
<EOS>
The
common

between
Calais
and

,
is
such
a
vehicle
as
no
man
would
use
,
who
has
any
regard
to
his
own
case
and

and
it

at

pace

an
English

.
  
<EOS>
In
ten
days
I
shall
set
out
on
my
journey
;
and
I
shall
leave
Boulogne
with

.
<EOS>
I
have
been
happy
in
the

of
Mrs
B
 
,
and
a

British
families
in
the
place
;
and

to
meet
here

,
whom

formerly

in
Paris
,
as
well

with
some

countrymen
,
officers
in

service

France
.
<EOS>
My
next
will
be
from
Paris
.
<EOS>
Remember
me
to
our
friends
at
A
 
'
s.
<EOS>
I
am
a

heavy
-
hearted
at
the
prospect

removing
to
such

distance
from

.
<EOS>
It

whether

shall
ever

.
<EOS>
My
health
is
very

.
<EOS>
Adieu
.
   
<EOS>
PARIS
,
October
12
,
1763
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
Of
our
journey
from
Boulogne
<EOS>
I
have
little
to
say
.
<EOS>
The
weather
was
favourable
,
and
the
roads
were
in
tolerable
order
.
<EOS>
We
found
good
accommodation
at
Montreuil
and
Amiens
;
but
in
every
other
place
where
we
stopped
,
we
met
with
abundance
of
dirt
,
and
the
most
flagrant
imposition
.
<EOS>
I
shall
not
pretend
to
describe
the
cities
of
Abbeville
and
Amiens
,
which
we
saw
only
en
passant
;
nor
take
up
your
time
with
an
account
of
the
stables
and
palace
of
Chantilly
,
belonging
to
the
prince
of
Conde
,
which
we
visited
the
last
day
of
our
journey
;
nor
shall
I
detain
you
with
a
detail
of
the
Trefors
de
St
Denis
,
which
,
together
with
the
tombs
in
the
abbey
church
,
afforded
us
some
amusement
while
our
dinner
was
getting
ready
.
<EOS>
All
these
particulars
are
mentioned
in
twenty
different
books
of
tours
,
travels
,
and
directions
,
which
you
have
often
perused
.
<EOS>
I
shall
only
observe
,
that
the
abbey
church
is
the
lightest
piece
of
Gothic
architecture
I
have
seen
,
and
the
air
within
seems
perfectly
free
from
that
damp
and
moisture
,
so
perceivable
in
all
our
old
cathedrals
.
<EOS>
This
must
be
owing
to
the
nature
of
its
situation
.
<EOS>
There
are
some
fine
marble
statues
that
adorn
the
tombs
of
certain
individuals
here
interred
;
but
they
are
mostly
in
the
French
taste
,
which
is
quite
contrary
to
the
simplicity
of
the
antients
.
<EOS>
Their
attitudes
are
affected
,
unnatural
,
and
desultory
;
and
their
draperies
fantastic
;
or
,
as
one
of
our
English
artists
expressed
himself
,
they
are
all
of
a
flutter
.
<EOS>
As
for
the
treasures
,
which
are
shewn
on
certain
days
to
the
populace
gratis
,
they
are
contained
in
a
number
of
presses
,
or
armoires
,
and
,
if
the
stones
are
genuine
,
they
must
be
inestimable
:
but
this
I
can
not
believe
.
<EOS>
Indeed
I
have
been
told
,
that
what
they
shew
as
diamonds
are
no
more
than
composition
:
nevertheless
,
exclusive
of
these
,
there
are
some
rough
stones
of
great
value
,
and
many
curiosities
worth
seeing
.
<EOS>
The
monk
that
shewed
them
was
the
very
image
of
our
friend
Hamilton
,
both
in
his
looks
and
manner
.
  
<EOS>
I
have
one
thing
very
extraordinary
to
observe
of
the
French
auberges
,
which
seems
to
be
a
remarkable
deviation
from
the
general
character
of
the
nation
.
<EOS>
The
landlords
,
hostesses
,
and
servants
of
the
inns
upon
the
road
,
have
not
the
least
dash
of
complaisance
in
their
behaviour

strangers
.
<EOS>
Instead

coming
to
the
door
,
to
receive
you
as
in
England
,
they
take
no
manner
of
notice
of
you
;
but
leave

to
find

enquire

way

the
kitchen
,
and
there

must
ask
several

for
a
chamber
,
before
they
seem

to
conduct

up
stairs
.
<EOS>
In
general
,
you
are
served
with
the
appearance
of
the
most
mortifying
indifference
,
at
the
very

they
are
laying

for
fleecing

.
<EOS>
It
is
a
very
odd
contrast
between
France
and
England
;
in
the
former
all

people
are
complaisant
but
the
publicans
;
in

any

but

the
publicans
.
<EOS>
When

said
all
the
people
in
France
,
I
ought

to
except
those
vermin
who
examine
the

.
<EOS>
Although

were
sealed

,

from

douane
,

the

;
and

to

,

,

.
  
<EOS>
I

,
in
the
Fauxbourg
St
Germain
;
and

the
Hotel

,

,

.
<EOS>
I

have
put
up
with
it
<EOS>

;

,

.
<EOS>
I

another

,

.
<EOS>
Besides

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

;

.
<EOS>

<EOS>
What

?
<EOS>

<EOS>
MADAM

<EOS>
I
shall
be
much
pleased
if
the
remarks
I
have
made
on
the
characters
of
the
French
people
,
can
afford
you
the
satisfaction
you
require
.
<EOS>
With
respect
to
the
ladies
I
can
only
judge
from
their
exteriors
:
but
,
indeed
,
these
are
so
characteristic
,
that
one
can
hardly
judge
amiss
;
unless
we
suppose
that
a
woman
of

and

may
be
so

by
the
absurdity
of
what
is
called

,
as
to
reject

,
and
disguise

,
in

.
<EOS>
That

may

,

.
<EOS>
I

known

,

,

that

,

.
<EOS>
I

not

a
French

's
dress
.
<EOS>
These

much

this

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
It

<EOS>
You

well

,

.
<EOS>
I

only
mention

dress
essential

the
fashion
in
this

,
which
seems

carry

;

,

<EOS>
When

<EOS>
Those

,

<EOS>
It

,

<EOS>
I

<EOS>
As

,

<EOS>
You

<EOS>
Ladies

<EOS>
As

<EOS>
Powder

<EOS>
In

<EOS>
The

,

<EOS>
On

<EOS>
This

,

<EOS>
It

"

<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
I
was
favoured
with
yours
at
Paris
,
and
look

your
reproaches

.
<EOS>
The

,
I

I

hitherto

,

<EOS>
With

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

a
very
large
laced
hat
,
and
a
meagre
,
wrinkled
,
discontented
countenance
.
  
<EOS>
The
fourth
night
of
our
journey
we
lay
at
Macon
,
and
the
next
day
passed
through
the
Lyonnois
,
which
is
a
fine
country
,
full
of
towns
,
villages
,
and
gentlemen
's
houses
.
<EOS>
In
passing
through
the
Maconnois
,
we
saw
a
great
many
fields
of
Indian
corn
,
which
grows
to
the
height
of
six
or
seven
feet
:
it
is
made
into
flour
for
the
use
of
the
common
people
,
and
goes
by
the
name
of
Turkey
wheat
.
<EOS>
Here
likewise
,
as
well
as
in
Dauphine
,
they
raise
a
vast
quantity
of
very
large
pompions
,
with
the
contents
of
which
they
thicken
their
soup
and
ragouts
.
  
<EOS>
As
we
travelled
only
while
the
sun
was
up
,
on
account
of
my
ill
health
,
and
the
post
horses
in
France
are
in
bad
order
,
we
seldom
exceeded
twenty
leagues
a
day
.
  
<EOS>
I
was
directed
to
a
lodging
-
house
at
Lyons
,
which
being
full
they
shewed
us
to
a
tavern
,
where
I
was
led
up
three
pair
of
stairs
,
to
an
apartment
consisting
of
three
paltry
chambers
,
for
which
the
people
demanded
twelve
livres
a
day
:
for
dinner
and
supper
they
asked
thirty
-
two
,
besides
three
livres
for
my
servant
;
so
that
my
daily
expence
would
have
amounted
to
about
forty
-
seven
livres
,
exclusive
of
breakfast
and
coffee
in
the
afternoon
.
<EOS>
I
was
so
provoked
at
this
extortion
,
that
,
without
answering
one
word
,
I
drove
to
another
auberge
,
where
I
now
am
,
and
pay
at
the
rate
of
two
-
and
-
thirty
livres
a
day
,
for
which
I
am
very
badly
lodged
,
and
but
very
indifferently
entertained
.
<EOS>
I
mention
these
circumstances
to
give
you
an
idea
of
the
imposition
to
which
strangers
are
subject
in
this
country
.
<EOS>
It
must
be
owned
,
however
,
that
in
the
article
of
eating
,
I
might
save
half
the
money
by
going
to
the
public
ordinary
;
but
this
is
a
scheme
of
oeconomy
,
which
(
exclusive
of
other
disagreeable
circumstances
)
neither
my
own
health
,
nor
that
of
my
wife
permits
me
to
embrace
.
<EOS>
My
journey
from
Paris
to
Lyons
,
including
the
hire
of
the
coach
,
and
all
expences
on
the
road
,
has
cost
me
,
within
a
few
shillings
,
forty
loui'dores
.
<EOS>
From
Paris
our
baggage
(
though
not
plombe
)
was
not
once
examined
till
we
arrived
in
this
city
,
at
the
gate
of
which
we
were
questioned
by
one
of
the
searchers
,
who
,
being
tipt
with
half
a
crown
,
allowed
us
to
proceed
without
further
enquiry
,
  
<EOS>
I
purposed
to
stay
in
Lyons
until
I
should
receive
some
letters
I
expected
from
London
,
to
be
forwarded
by
my
banker
at
Paris
:
but
the
enormous
expence
of
living
in
this
manner
has
determined
me
to
set
out
in
a
day
or
two
for
Montpellier
,
although
that
place
is
a
good
way
out
of
the
road
to
Nice
.
<EOS>
My
reasons
for
taking
that
route
I
shall
communicate
in
my
next
.
<EOS>
Mean
-
while
,
I
am
ever
,
  
Dear
Sir
,
Your
affectionate
and
obliged
humble
servant
.
   
<EOS>
MONTPELLIER
,
November
5
,
1763
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
The
city
of
Lyons
has
been
so
often
and
so
circumstantially
described
,
that
I
can
not
pretend
to
say
any
thing
new
on
the
subject
.
<EOS>
Indeed
,
I
know
very
little
of
it
,
but
what
I
have
read
in
books
;
as
I
had
but
one
day
to
make
a
tour
of
the
streets
,
squares
,
and
other
remarkable
places
.
<EOS>
The
bridge
over
the
Rhone
seems
to
be
so
slightly
built
,
that
I
should
imagine
it
would
be
one
day
carried
away
by
that
rapid
river
;
especially
as
the
arches
are
so
small
,
that
,
after
great
rains
they
are
sometimes
bouchees
,
or
stopped
up
;
that
is
,
they
do
not
admit
a
sufficient
passage
for
the
encreased
body
of
the
water
.
<EOS>
In
order
to
remedy
this
dangerous
defect
,
in
some
measure
,
they
found
an
artist
some
years
ago
,
who
has
removed
a
middle
pier
,
and
thrown
two
arches
into
one
.
<EOS>
This
alteration
they
looked
upon
as
a
masterpiece
in
architecture
,
though
there
is
many
a
common
mason
in
England
,
who
would
have
undertaken
and
performed
the
work
,
without
valuing
himself
much
upon
the
enterprize
.
<EOS>
This
bridge
,
as
well
as
that
of
St
Esprit
,
is
built
,
not
in
a
strait
line
across
the
river
,
but
with
a
curve
,
which
forms
a
convexity
to
oppose
the
current
.
<EOS>
Such
a
bend
is
certainly
calculated
for
the
better
resisting
the
general
impetuosity
of
the
stream
,
and
has
no
bad
effect
to
the
eye
.
  
<EOS>
Lyons
is
a
great
,
populous
,
and
flourishing
city
but
I
am
surprised
to
find
it
is
counted
a
healthy
place
,
and
that
the
air
of
it
is
esteemed
favourable
to
pulmonic
disorders
.
<EOS>
It
is
situated
on
the
confluence
of
two
large
rivers
,
from
which
there
must
be
a
great
evaporation
,
as
well
as
from
the
low
marshy
grounds
,
which
these
rivers
often
overflow
.
<EOS>
This
must
render
the
air
moist
,
frouzy
,
and
even
putrid
,
if
it
was
not
well
ventilated
by
winds
from
the
mountains
of
Swisserland
;
and
in
the
latter
end
of
autumn
,
it
must
be
subject
to
fogs
.
<EOS>
The
morning
we
set
out
from
thence
,
the
whole
city
and
adjacent
plains
were
covered
with
so
thick
a
fog
,
that
we
could
not
distinguish
from
the
coach
the
head
of
the
foremost
mule
that
drew
it
.
<EOS>
Lyons
is
said
to
be
very
hot
in
summer
,
and
very
cold
in
winter
;
therefore
I
imagine
must
abound
with
inflammatory
and
intermittent
disorders
in
the
spring
and
fall
of
the
year
.
  
<EOS>
My
reasons
for
going
to
Montpellier
,
which
is
out
of
the
strait
road
to
Nice
,
were
these
.
<EOS>
Having
no
acquaintance
nor
correspondents
in
the
South
of
France
,
I
had
desired
my
credit
might
be
sent
to
the
same
house
to
which
my
heavy
baggage
was
consigned
.
<EOS>
I
expected
to
find
my
baggage
at
Cette
,
which
is
the
sea
-
port
of
Montpellier
;
and
there
I
also
hoped
to
find
a
vessel
,
in
which
I
might
be
transported
by
sea
to
Nice
,
without
further
trouble
.
<EOS>
I
longed
to
try
what
effect
the
boasted
air
of
Montpellier
would
have
upon
my
constitution
;
and
I
had
a
great
desire
to
see
the
famous
monuments
of
antiquity
in
and
about
the
ancient
city
of
Nismes
,
which
is
about
eight
leagues
short
of
Montpellier
.
  
<EOS>
At
the
inn
where
we
lodged
,
I
found
a
return
berline
,
belonging
to
Avignon
,
with
three
mules
,
which
are
the
animals
commonly
used
for
carriages
in
this
country
.
<EOS>
This
I
hired
for
five
loui'dores
.
<EOS>
The
coach
was
large
,
commodious
,
and
well
-
fitted
;
the
mules
were
strong
and
in
good
order
;
and
the
driver
,
whose
name
was
Joseph
,
appeared
to
be
a
sober
,
sagacious
,
intelligent
fellow
,
perfectly
well
acquainted
with
every
place
in
the
South
of
France
.
<EOS>
He
told
me
he
was
owner
of
the
coach
,
but
I
afterwards
learned
,
he
was
no
other
than
a
hired
servant
.
<EOS>
I
likewise
detected
him
in
some
knavery
,
in
the
course
of
our
journey
;
and
plainly
perceived
he
had
a
fellow
-
feeling
with
the
inn
-
keepers
on
the
road
;
but
,
in
other
respects
,
he
was
very
obliging
,
serviceable
,
and
even
entertaining
.
<EOS>
There
are
some
knavish
practices
of
this
kind
,
at
which
a
traveller
will
do
well
to
shut
his
eyes
,
for
his
own
ease
and
convenience
.
<EOS>
He
will
be
lucky
if
he
has
to
do
with
a
sensible
knave
,
like
Joseph
,
who
understood
his
interest
too
well
to
be
guilty
of
very
flagrant
pieces
of
imposition
.
  
<EOS>
A
man
,
impatient
to
be
at
his
journey
's
end
,
will
find
this
a
most
disagreeable
way
of
travelling
.
<EOS>
In
summer
it
must
be
quite
intolerable
.
<EOS>
The
mules
are
very
sure
,
but
very
slow
.
<EOS>
The
journey
seldom
exceeds
eight
leagues
,
about
four
and
twenty
miles
a
day
:
and
as
those
people
have
certain
fixed
stages
,
you
are
sometimes
obliged
to
rise
in
a
morning
before
day
;
a
circumstance
very
grievous
to
persons
in
ill
health
.
<EOS>
These
inconveniences
,
however
,
were
over
-
balanced
by
other
agreemens
.
<EOS>
We
no
,
sooner
quitted
Lyons
,
than
we
got
into
summer
weather
,
and
travelling
through
a
most
romantic
country
,
along
the
banks
of
the
Rhone
,
had
opportunities
(
from
the
slowness
of
our
pace
)
to
contemplate
its
beauties
at
leisure
.
  
<EOS>
The
rapidity
of
the
Rhone
is
,
in
a
great
measure
,
owing
to
its
being
confined
within
steep
banks
on
each
side
.
<EOS>
These
are
formed
almost
through
its
whole
course
,
by
a
double
chain
of
mountains
,
which
rise
with
all
abrupt
ascent
from
both
banks
of
the
river
.
<EOS>
The
mountains
are
covered
with
vineyards
,
interspersed
with
small
summer
-
houses
,
and
in
many
places
they
are
crowned
with
churches
,
chapels
,
and
convents
,
which
add
greatly
to
the
romantic
beauty
of
the
prospect
.
<EOS>
The
highroad
,
as
far
as
Avignon
,
lies
along
the
side
of
the
river
,
which
runs
almost
in
a
straight
line
,
and
affords
great
convenience
for
inland
commerce
.
<EOS>
Travellers
,
bound
to
the
southern
parts
of
France
,
generally
embark
in
the
diligence
at
Lyons
,
and
glide
down
this
river
with
great
velocity
,
passing
a
great
number
of
towns
and
villages
on
each
side
,
where
they
find
ordinaries
every
day
at
dinner
and
supper
.
<EOS>
In
good
weather
,
there
is
no
danger
in
this
method
of
travelling
,
 
'
till
you
come
to
the
Pont
St
Esprit
,
where
the
stream
runs
through
the
arches
with
such
rapidity
,
that
the
boat
is
sometimes
overset
.
<EOS>
But
those
passengers
who
are
under
any
apprehension
are
landed
above
-
bridge
,
and
taken
in
again
,
after
the
boat
has
passed
,
just
in
the
same
manner
as
at
London
Bridge
.
<EOS>
The
boats
that
go
up
the
river
are
drawn
against
the
stream
by
oxen
,
which
swim
through
one
of
the
arches
of
this
bridge
,
the
driver
sitting
between
the
horns
of
the
foremost
beast
.
<EOS>
We
set
out
from
Lyons
early
on
Monday
morning
,
and
as
a
robbery
had
been
a
few
days
before
committed
in
that
neighbourhood
,
I
ordered
my
servant
to
load
my
musquetoon
with
a
charge
of
eight
balls
.
<EOS>
By
the
bye
,
this
piece
did
not
fail
to
attract
the
curiosity
and
admiration
of
the
people
in
every
place
through
which
we
passed
.
<EOS>
The
carriage
no
sooner
halted
,
than
a
crowd
immediately
surrounded
the
man
to
view
the
blunderbuss
,
which
they
dignified
with
the
title
of
petit
canon
.
<EOS>
At
Nuys
in
Burgundy
,
he
fired
it
in
the
air
,
and
the
whole
mob
dispersed
,
and
scampered
off
like
a
flock
of
sheep
.
<EOS>
In
our
journey
hither
,
we
generally
set
out
in
a
morning
at
eight
o'clock
,
and
travelled
 
'
till
noon
,
when
the
mules
were
put
up
and
rested
a
couple
of
hours
.
<EOS>
During
this
halt
,
Joseph
went
to
dinner
,
and
we
went
to
breakfast
,
after
which
we
ordered
provision
for
our
refreshment
in
the
coach
,
which
we
took
about
three
or
four
in
the
afternoon
,
halting
for
that
purpose
,
by
the
side
of
some
transparent
brook
,
which
afforded
excellent
water
to
mix
with
our
wine
.
<EOS>
In
this
country
I
was
almost
poisoned
with
garlic
,
which
they
mix
in
their
ragouts
,
and
all
their
sauces
;
nay
,
the
smell
of
it
perfumes
the
very
chambers
,
as
well
as
every
person
you
approach
.
<EOS>
I
was
also
very
sick
of
been
ficas
,
grives
,
or
thrushes
,
and
other
little
birds
,
which
are
served
up
twice
a
day
at
all
ordinaries
on
the
road
.
<EOS>
They
make
their
appearance
in
vine
-
leaves
,
and
are
always
half
raw
,
in
which
condition
the
French
choose
to
eat
them
,
rather
than
run
the
risque
of
losing
the
juice
by
over
-
roasting
.
  
<EOS>
The
peasants
on
the
South
of
France
are
poorly
clad
,
and
look
as
if
they
were
half
-
starved
,
diminutive
,
swarthy
,
and
meagre
;
and
yet
the
common
people
who
travel
,
live
luxuriously
on
the
road
.
<EOS>
Every
carrier
and
mule
-
driver
has
two
meals
a
day
,
consisting
each
of
a
couple
of
courses
and
a
dessert
,
with
tolerable
small
wine
.
<EOS>
That
which
is
called
hermitage
,
and
grows
in
this
province
of
Dauphine
,
is
sold
on
the
spot
for
three
livres
a
bottle
.
<EOS>
The
common
draught
,
which
you
have
at
meals
in
this
country
,
is
remarkably
strong
,
though
in
flavour
much
inferior
to
that
of
Burgundy
.
<EOS>
The
accommodation
is
tolerable
,
though
they
demand
(
even
in
this
cheap
country
)
<EOS>
the
exorbitant
price
of
four
livres
a
head
for
every
meal
,
of
those
who
choose
to
eat
in
their
own
apartments
.
<EOS>
I
insisted
,
however
,
upon
paying
them
with
three
,
which
they
received
,
though
not
without
murmuring
and
seeming
discontented
.
<EOS>
In
this
journey
,
we
found
plenty
of
good
mutton
,
pork
,
poultry
,
and
game
,
including
the
red
partridge
,
which
is
near
twice

,

.
<EOS>
Six

.
<EOS>
Indeed

<EOS>

<EOS>
So

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

.
