<EOS>
This
expedient
would
not
answer
in
a
country
subject
to
rain
and
storms
;
but
here
,
where
there
is
very
little
of
either
,
the
paper
lozenges
answer
tolerably
well
.
<EOS>
The
bourgeois
,
however
,
begin
to
have
their
houses
sashed
with
glass
.
<EOS>
Between
the
town
-
wall
and
the
sea
,
the
fishermen
haul
up
their
boats
upon
the
open
beach
;
but
on
the
other
side
of
the
rock
,
where
the
castle
stood
,
is
the
port
or
harbour
of
Nice
,
upon
which
some
money
has
been
expended
.
<EOS>
It
is
a
small
basin
,
defended
to
seaward
by
a
mole
of
free
-
stone
,
which
is
much
better
contrived
than
executed
:
for
the
sea
has
already
made
three
breaches
in
it
;
and
in
all
probability
,
in
another
winter
,
the
extremity
of
it
will
be
carried
quite
away
.
<EOS>
It
would
require
the
talents
of
a
very
skilful
architect
to
lay
the
foundation
of
a
good
mole
,
on
an
open
beach
like
this
;
exposed
to
the
swell
of
the
whole
Mediterranean
,
without
any
island
or
rock
in
the
offing
,
to
break
the
force
of
the
waves
.
<EOS>
Besides
,
the
shore
is
bold
,
and
the
bottom
foul
.
<EOS>
There
are
seventeen
feet
of
water
in
the
basin
,
sufficient
to
float
vessels
of
one
hundred
and
fifty
ton
;
and
this
is
chiefly
supplied
by
a
small
stream
of
very
fine
water
;
another
great
convenience
for
shipping
.
<EOS>
On
the
side
of
the
mole
,
there
is
a
constant
guard
of
soldiers
,
and
a
battery
of
seven
cannon
,
pointing
to
the
sea
.
<EOS>
On
the
other
side
,
there
is
a
curious
manufacture
for
twisting
or
reeling
silk
;
a
tavern
,
a
coffee
-
house
,
and
several
other
buildings
,
for
the
convenience
of
the
sea
-
faring
people
.
<EOS>
Without
the
harbour
,
is
a
lazarette
,
where
persons
coming
from
infected
places
,
are
obliged
to
perform
quarantine
.
<EOS>
The
harbour
has
been
declared
a
free
-
port
,
and
it
is
generally
full
of
tartans
,
polacres
,
and
other
small
vessels
,
that
come
from
Sardinia
,
Ivica
,
Italy
,
and
Spain
,
loaded
with
salt
,
wine
,
and
other
commodities
;
but
here
is
no
trade
of
any
great
consequence
.
  
<EOS>
The
city
of
Nice
is
provided
with
a
senate
,
which
administers
justice
under
the
auspices
of
an
avocat
-
general
,
sent
hither
by
the
king
.
<EOS>
The
internal
oeconomy
of
the
town
is
managed
by
four
consuls
;
one
for
the
noblesse
.
<EOS>
another
for
the
merchants
,
a
third
for
the
bourgeois
,
and
a
fourth
for
the
peasants
.
<EOS>
These
are
chosen
annually
from
the
town
-
council
.
<EOS>
They
keep
the
streets
and
markets
in
order
,
and
superintend
the
public
works
.
<EOS>
There
is
also
an
intendant
,
who
takes
care
of
his
majesty
's
revenue
:
but
there
is
a
discretionary
power
lodged
in
the
person
of
the
commandant
,
who
is
always
an
officer
of
rank
in
the
service
,
and
has
under
his
immediate
command
the
regiment
which
is
here
in
garrison
.
<EOS>
That
which
is
here
now
is
a
Swiss
battalion
,
of
which
the
king
has
five
or
six
in
his
service
.
<EOS>
There
is
likewise
a
regiment
of
militia
,
which
is
exercised
once
a
year
.
<EOS>
But
of
all
these
particulars
,
I
shall
speak
more
fully
on
another
occasion
.
  
<EOS>
When
I
stand
upon
the
rampart
,
and
look
round
me
,
I
can
scarce
help
thinking
myself
inchanted
.
<EOS>
The
small
extent
of
country
which
I
see
,
is
all
cultivated
like
a
garden
.
<EOS>
Indeed
,
the
plain
presents
nothing
but
gardens
,
full
of
green
trees
,
loaded
with
oranges
,
lemons
,
citrons
,
and
bergamots
,
which
make
a
delightful
appearance
.
<EOS>
If
you
examine
them
more
nearly
,
you
will
find
plantations
of
green
pease
ready
to
gather
;
all
sorts
of
sallading
,
and
pot
-
herbs
,
in
perfection
;
and
plats
of
roses
,
carnations
,
ranunculas
,
anemonies
,
and
daffodils
,
blowing
in
full
glory
,
with
such
beauty
,
vigour
,
and
perfume
,
as
no
flower
in
England
ever
exhibited
.
  
<EOS>
I
must
tell
you
,
that
presents
of
carnations
are
sent
from
hence
,
in
the
winter
,
to
Turin
and
Paris
;
nay
,
sometimes
as
far
as
London
,
by
the
post
.
<EOS>
They
are
packed
up
in
a
wooden
box
,
without
any
sort
of
preparation
,
one
pressed
upon
another
:
the
person
who
receives
them
,
cuts
off
a
little
bit
of
the
stalk
,
and
steeps
them
for
two
hours
in
vinegar
and
water
,
when
they
recover
their
full
bloom
and
beauty
.
<EOS>
Then
he
places
them
in
water
-
bottles
,
in
an
apartment
where
they
are
screened
from
the
severities
of
the
weather
;
and
they
will
continue
fresh
and
unfaded
the
best
part
of
a
month
.
  
<EOS>
Amidst
the
plantations
in
the
neighbourhood
of
Nice
,
appear
a
vast
number
of
white
bastides
,
or
country
-
houses
,
which
make
a
dazzling
shew
.
<EOS>
Some
few
of
these
are
good
villas
,
belonging
to
the
noblesse
of
this
county
;
and
even
some
of
the
bourgeois
are
provided
with
pretty
lodgeable
cassines
;
but
in
general
,
they
are
the
habitations
of
the
peasants
,
and
contain
nothing
but
misery
and
vermin
.
<EOS>
They
are
all
built
square
;
and
,
being
whitened
with
lime
or
plaister
,
contribute
greatly
to
the
richness
of
the
view
.
<EOS>
The
hills
are
shaded
to
the
tops
with
olive
-
trees
,
which
are
always
green
;
and
those
hills
are
over
-
topped
by
more
distant
mountains
,
covered
with
snow
.
<EOS>
When
I
turn
myself
towards
the
sea
,
the
view
is
bounded
by
the
horizon
;
yet
in
a
clear
morning
,
one
can
perceive
the
high
lands
of
Corsica
.
<EOS>
On
the
right
hand
,
it
is
terminated
by
Antibes
,
and
the
mountain
of
Esterelles
,
which
I
described
in
my
last
.
<EOS>
As
for
the
weather
,
you
will
conclude
,
from
what
I
have
said
of
the
oranges
,
flowers
,
etc
.
<EOS>
that
it
must
be
wonderfully
mild
and
serene
:
<EOS>
but
of
the
climate
,
I
shall
speak
hereafter
.
<EOS>
Let
me
only
observe
,
en
passant
,
that
the
houses
in
general
have
no
chimnies
,
but
in
their
kitchens
;
and
that
many
people
,
even
of
condition
,
at
Nice
,
have
no
fire
in
their
chambers
,
during
the
whole
winter
.
<EOS>
When
the
weather
happens
to
be
a
little
more
sharp
than
usual
,
they
warm
their
apartments
with
a
brasiere
or
pan
of
charcoal
.
  
<EOS>
Though
Nice
itself
retains
few
marks
of
antient
splendor
,
there
are
considerable
monuments
of
antiquity
in
its
neighbourhood
.
<EOS>
About
two
short
miles
from
the
town
,
upon
the
summit
of
a
pretty
high
hill
,
we
find
the
ruins
of
the
antient
city
Cemenelion
,
now
called
Cimia
,
which
was
once
the
metropolis
of
the
Maritime
Alps
,
and
the
scat
of
a
Roman
president
.
<EOS>
With
respect
to
situation
,
nothing
could
be
more
agreeable
or
salubrious
.
<EOS>
It
stood
upon
the
gentle
ascent
and
summit
of
a
hill
,
fronting
the
Mediterranean
;
from
the
shore
of
which
,
it
is
distant
about
half
a
league
;
and
,
on
the
other
side
,
it
overlooked
a
bottom
,
or
narrow
vale
,
through
which
the
Paglion
(
antiently
called
Paulo
)
<EOS>
runs
towards
the
walls
of
Nice
.
<EOS>
It
was
inhabited
by
a
people
,
whom
Ptolomy
and
Pliny
call
the
Vedantij
:
but
<EOS>
these
were
undoubtedly
mixed
with
a
Roman
colony
,
as
appears
by
the
monuments
which
still
remain
;
I
mean
the
ruins
of
an
amphitheatre
,
a
temple
of
Apollo
,
baths
,
aqueducts
,
sepulchral
,
and
other
stones
,
with
inscriptions
,
and
a
great
number
of
medals
which
the
peasants
have
found
by
accident
,
in
digging
and
labouring
the
vineyards
and
cornfields
,
which
now
cover
the
ground
where
the
city
stood
.
  
<EOS>
Touching
this
city
,
very
little
is
to
be
learned
from
the
antient
historians
:
but
that
it
was
the
seat
of
a
Roman
praeses
,
is
proved
by
the
two
following
inscriptions
,
which
are
still
extant
.

<EOS>
Inscribed
by

,

Patron
,

,

of

for
the

of

,

,

Corn

,

in

,

,
discovering
and

the
water
to

for

of

.
  
<EOS>
This
president
well

such

from
a

assisted
in

,

and

.
<EOS>
You

of

,

,

the

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,
[

;

.
]
 
<EOS>
Chariots
,
Pursuivants
,
ivory
staff
,
and
chair
of
state
.
  
<EOS>
I
shall
give

one
more

inscription
on

,

the

to
the

of
St

,

,

.
<EOS>
This

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

.
<EOS>
Charles

,

and

,

.

<EOS>
Freely
consecrated
by
Aurelius
Rhodismanus
,
the
Emperor
's
Freedman
,
to
the
much
honoured
memory
of
his
dear
Consort
Flavia
Aurelia
of
Rome
,
a
woman
equally
distinguished
by
her
unblemished
Virtue
and
conjugal
affection
.
<EOS>
His
children
Martial
and
Aurelia
Romula
deeply
affected
and
distressed
by
the
Violence
of
his
Grief
,
erected
and
dedicated
a
monument
to
their
dear
deserving
Parent
.
 
<EOS>
[
I
do
n't
pretend
to
translate
these
inscriptions
literally
,
because
I
am

the
meaning

.
]
  
<EOS>
The
amphitheatre
of
Cemenelion
is
but
very
small
,
compared
to
that
of
Nismes
.
<EOS>
The
arena
is
ploughed
up
,
and
bears
corn
:
some
of
the
seats
remain
,
and
part
of
two
opposite
porticos
;
but
all
the
columns
,
and
the
external
facade
of
the
building
,
are
taken
away
so
that
it
is
impossible
to
judge
of
the
architecture
,
all
we
can
perceive
is
,
that
it
was
built
in
an
oval
form
.
<EOS>
About
one
hundred
paces
from
the
amphitheatre
stood
an
antient
temple
,
supposed
to
have
been
dedicated
to
Apollo
.
<EOS>
The
original
roof
is
demolished
,
as
well
as
the
portico
;
the
vestiges
of
which
may
still
be
traced
.
<EOS>
The
part
called
the
Basilica
,
and
about
one
half
of
the
Cella
Sanctior
,
remain
,
and
are
converted
into
the
dwelling
-
house
and
stable
of
the
peasant
who
takes
care
of
the
count
de
Gubernatis
's
garden
,
in
which
this
monument
stands
.
<EOS>
In
the
Cella
Sanctior
,
I
found
a
lean
cow
,
a
he
-
goat
,
and
a
jack
-
ass
;
the
very
same
conjunction
of
animals
which
I
had
seen
drawing
a
plough
in
Burgundy
.
<EOS>
Several
mutilated
statues
have
been
dug
up
from
the
ruins
of
this

;
and
a
great
number
of
medals
have
been
found
in
the
different
vineyards
which
now
occupy
the
space
upon
which
stood
the
antient
city
of
Cemenelion
.
<EOS>
These
were
of
gold
,
silver
,
and
brass
.
<EOS>
Many
of
them
were
presented
to
Charles
Emanuel
I
,
duke
of
Savoy
.
<EOS>
The
prince
of
Monaco
has
a
good
number
of
them
in
his
collection
;
and
the
rest
are
in
private
hands
.
<EOS>
The
peasants
,
in
digging
,
have
likewise
found
many
urns
,
lachrymatories
,
and
sepulchral
stones
,
with
epitaphs
,
which
are
now
dispersed
among
different
convents
and
private
houses
.
<EOS>
All
this
ground
is
a
rich
mine
of
antiquities
,
which
,
if
properly
worked
,
would
produce
a
great
number
of
valuable
curiosities
.
<EOS>
Just
by
the
temple
of
Apollo
were
the
ruins
of
a
bath
,
composed
of
great
blocks
of
marble
,
which
have
been
taken
away
for
the
purposes
of
modern
building
.
<EOS>
In
all
probability
,
many
other
noble
monuments
of
this
city
have
been
dilapidated
by
the
same
barbarous
oeconomy
.
<EOS>
There
are
some
subterranean
vaults
,
through
which
the
water
was
conducted
to
this
bath
,
still
extant
in
the
garden
of
the
count
de
Gubernatis
.
<EOS>
Of
the
aqueduct
that
conveyed
water
to
the
town
,
I
can
say
very
little
,
but
that
it
was
scooped
through
a
mountain
:
that
this
subterranean
passage
was
discovered
some
years
ago
,
by
removing
the
rubbish
which
choaked
it
up
:
that
the
people
penetrating
a
considerable
way
,
by
the
help
of
lighted
torches
,
found
a
very
plentiful
stream
of
water
flowing
in
an
aqueduct
,
as
high
as
an
ordinary
man
,
arched
over
head
,
and
lined
with
a
sort
of
cement
.
<EOS>
They
could
not
,
however
,
trace
this
stream
to
its
source
;
and
it
is
again
stopped
up
with
earth
and
rubbish
.
<EOS>
There
is
not
a
soul
in
this
country
,
who
has
either
spirit
or
understanding
to
conduct
an
inquiry
of
this
kind
.
<EOS>
Hard
by
the
amphitheatre
is
a
convent
of
Recollets
,
built
in
a
very
romantic
situation
,
on
the
brink
of
a
precipice
.
<EOS>
On
one
side
of
their
garden
,
they
ascend
to
a
kind
of
esplanade
,
which
they
say
was
part
of
the
citadel
of
Cemenelion
.
<EOS>
They
have
planted
it
with
cypress
-
trees
,
and
flowering
-
shrubs
.
<EOS>
One
of
the
monks
told
me
,
that
it
is
vaulted
below
,
as
they
can
plainly
perceive
by
the
sound
of
their
instruments
used
in
houghing
the
ground
.
<EOS>
A
very
small
expence
would
bring
the
secrets
of
this
cavern
to
light
.
<EOS>
They
have
nothing
to
do
,
but
to
make
a
breach
in
the
wall
,
which
appears
uncovered
towards
the
garden
.
  
<EOS>
The
city
of
Cemenelion
was
first
sacked
by
the
Longobards
,
who
made
an
irruption
into
Provence
,
under
their
king
Alboinus
,
about
the
middle
of
the
sixth
century
.
<EOS>
It
was
afterwards
totally
destroyed
by
the
Saracens
,
who
,
at
different
times
,
ravaged
this
whole
coast
.
<EOS>
The
remains
of
the
people
are
supposed
to
have
changed
their
habitation
,
and
formed
a
coalition
with
the
inhabitants
of
Nice
.
  
<EOS>
What
further
I
have
to
say
of
Nice
,
you
shall
know
in
good
time
;
at
present
,
I
have
nothing
to
add
,
but
what
you
very
well
know
,
that
I
am
always
your
affectionate
humble
servant
.
   
<EOS>
NICE
,
January
20
,
1764
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
Last
Sunday
I
crossed
Montalban
on
horseback
,
with
some
Swiss
officers
,
on
a
visit
to
our
consul
,
Mr
B
 
d
,
who
lives
at
Ville
Franche
,
about
half
a
league
from
Nice
.
<EOS>
It
is
a
small
town
,
built
upon
the
side
of
a
rock
,
at
the
bottom
of
the
harbour
,
which
is
a
fine
basin
,
surrounded
with
hills
on
every
side
,
except
to
the
south
,
where
it
lies
open
to
the
sea
.
<EOS>
If
there
was
a
small
island
in
the
mouth
of
it
,
to
break
off
the
force
of
the
waves
,
when
the
wind
is
southerly
,
it
would
be
one
of
the
finest
harbours
in
the
world
;
for
the
ground
is
exceeding
good
for
anchorage
:
there
is
a
sufficient
depth
of
water
,
and
room
enough
for
the
whole
navy
of
England
.
<EOS>
On
the
right
hand
,
as
you
enter
the
port
,
there
is
an
elegant
fanal
,
or
lighthouse
,
kept
in
good
repair
:
but
in
all
the
charts
of
this
coast
which
I
have
seen
,
this
lanthorn
is
laid
down
to
the
westward
of
the
harbour
;
an
error
equally
absurd
and
dangerous
,
as
it
may
mislead
the
navigator
,
and
induce
him
to
run
his
ship
among
the
rocks
,
to
the
eastward
of
the
lighthouse
,
where
it
would
undoubtedly
perish
.
<EOS>
Opposite
to
the
mouth
of
the
harbour
is
the
fort
,
which
can
be
of
no
service
,
but
in
defending
the
shipping
and
the
town
by
sea
;
for
,
by
land
,
it
is
commanded
by
Montalban
,
and
all
the
hills
in
the
neighbourhood
.
<EOS>
In
the
war
of
1744
,
it
was
taken
and
retaken
.
<EOS>
At
present
,
it
is
in
tolerable
good
repair
.
<EOS>
On
the
left
of
the
fort
,
is
the
basin
for
the
gallies
,
with
a
kind
of
dock
,
in
which
they
are
built
,
and
occasionally
laid
up
to
be
refitted
.
<EOS>
This
basin
is
formed
by
a
pretty
stone
mole
;
and
here
his
Sardinian
majesty
's
two
gallies
lie
perfectly
secure
,
moored
with
their
sterns
close
to
the
jette
.
<EOS>
I
went
on
board
one
of
these
vessels
,
and
saw
about
two
hundred
miserable
wretches
,
chained
to
the
banks
on
which
they
sit
and
row
,
when
the
galley
is
at
sea
.
<EOS>
This
is
a
sight
which
a
British
subject
,
sensible
of
the
blessing
he
enjoys
,
can
not
behold
without
horror
and
compassion
.
<EOS>
Not
but
that
if
we
consider
the
nature
of
the
case
,
with
coolness
and
deliberation
,
we
must
acknowledge
the
justice
,
and
even
sagacity
,
of
employing
for
the
service
of
the
public
,
those
malefactors
who
have
forfeited
their
title
to
the
privileges
of
the
community
.
<EOS>
Among
the
slaves
at
Ville
Franche
is
a
Piedmontese
count
,
condemned
to
the
gallies
for
life
,
in
consequence
of
having
been
convicted
of
forgery
.
<EOS>
He
is
permitted
to
live
on
shore
;
and
gets
money
by
employing
the
other
slaves
to
knit
stockings
for
sale
.
<EOS>
He
appears
always
in
the
Turkish
habit
,
and
is
in
a
fair
way
of
raising
a
better
fortune
than
that
which
he
has
forfeited
.
  
<EOS>
It
is
a
great
pity
,
however
,
and
a
manifest
outrage
against
the
law
of
nations
,
as
well
as
of
humanity
,
to
mix
with
those
banditti
,
the
Moorish
and
Turkish
prisoners
who
are
taken
in
the
prosecution
of
open
war
.
<EOS>
It
is
certainly
no
justification
of
this
barbarous
practice
,
that
the
Christian
prisoners
are
treated
as
cruelly
at
Tunis
and
Algiers
.
<EOS>
It
would
be
for
the
honour
of
Christendom
,
to
set
an
example
of
generosity
to
the
Turks
;
and
,
if
they
would
not
follow
it
,
to
join
their
naval
forces
,
and
extirpate
at
once
those
nests
of
pirates
,
who
have
so
long
infested
the
Mediterranean
.
<EOS>
Certainly
,
nothing
can
be
more
shameful
,
than
the
treaties
which
France
and
the
Maritime
Powers
have
concluded
with
those
barbarians
.
<EOS>
They
supply
them
with
artillery
,
arms
,
and
ammunition
,
to
disturb
their
neighbours
.
<EOS>
They
even
pay
them
a
sort
of
tribute
,
under
the
denomination
of
presents
;
and
often
put
up
with
insults
tamely
,
for
the
sordid
consideration
of
a
little
gain
in
the
way
of
commerce
.
<EOS>
They
know
that
Spain
,
Sardinia
,
and
almost
all
the
Catholic
powers
in
the
Mediterranean
,
Adriatic
,
and
Levant
,
are
at
perpetual
war
with
those
Mahometans
;
that
while
Algiers
,
Tunis
,
and
Sallee
,
maintain
armed
cruisers
at
sea
,
those
Christian
powers
will
not
run
the
risque
of
trading
in
their
own
bottoms
,
but
rather
employ
as
carriers
the
maritime
nations
,
who
are
at
peace
with
the
infidels
.
<EOS>
It
is
for
our
share
of
this
advantage
,
that
we
cultivate
the
piratical
States
of
Barbary
,
and
meanly
purchase
passports
of
them
,
thus
acknowledging
them
masters
of
the
Mediterranean
.
  
<EOS>
The
Sardinian
gallies
are
mounted
each
with
five
-
and
-
twenty
oars
,
and
six
guns
,
six
-
pounders
,
of
a
side
,
and
a
large
piece
of
artillery
amidships
,
pointing
ahead
,
which
(
so
far
as
I
am
able
to
judge
)
can
never
be
used
point
-
blank
,
without
demolishing
the
head
or
prow
of
the
galley
.
<EOS>
The
accommodation
on
board
for
the
officers
is
wretched
.
<EOS>
There
is
a
paltry
cabin
in
the
poop
for
the
commander
;
but
all
the
other
officers
lie
below
the
slaves
,
in
a
dungeon
,
where
they
have
neither
light
,
air
,
nor
any
degree
of
quiet
;
half
suffocated
by
the
heat
of
the
place
;
tormented
by
fleas
,
bugs
,
and
lice
;
and
disturbed
by
the
incessant
noise
over
head
.
<EOS>
The
slaves
lie
upon
the
naked
banks
,
without
any
other
covering
than
a
tilt
.
<EOS>
This
,
however
,
is
no
great
hardship
,
in
a
climate
where
there
is
scarce
any
winter
.
<EOS>
They
are
fed
with
a
very
scanty
allowance
of
bread
,
and
about
fourteen
beans
a
day
and
twice
a
week
<EOS>
they
have
a
little
rice
,
or
cheese
,
but
most
of
them
,
while
they
are
in
harbour
knit
stockings
,
or
do
some
other
kind
of
work
,
which
enables
them
to
make
some
addition
to
this
wretched
allowance
.
<EOS>
When
they
happen
to
be
at
sea
in
bad
weather
,
their
situation
is
truly
deplorable
.
<EOS>
Every
wave
breaks
over
the
vessel
,
and
not
only
keeps
them
continually
wet
,
but
comes
with
such
force
,
that
they
are
dashed
against
the
banks
with
surprising
violence
:
sometimes
their
limbs
are
broke
,
and
sometimes
their
brains
dashed
out
.
<EOS>
It
is
impossible
(
they
say
)
to
keep
such
a
number
of
desperate
people
under
any
regular
command
,
without
exercising
such
severities
as
must
shock
humanity
.
<EOS>
It
is
almost
equally
impossible
to
maintain
any
tolerable
degree
of
cleanliness
,
where
such
a
number
of
wretches
are
crouded
together
without
conveniences
,
or
even
the
necessaries
of
life
.
<EOS>
They
are
ordered
twice
a
week
to
strip
,
clean
,
and
bathe
themselves
in
the
sea
:
but
,
notwithstanding
all
the
precautions
of
discipline
,
they
swarm
with
vermin
,
and
the
vessel
smells
like
an
hospital

,

<EOS>

NICE

.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
DOCTOR
,
 
A
few
days
ago
,
I
rode
out
with
two
gentlemen
of
this
country
,
to
see
a
stream
of
water
which
was
formerly
conveyed
in
an
aqueduct
to
the
antient
city
of
Cemenelion
,
from
whence
this
place
is
distant
about
a
mile
,
though
separated
by
abrupt

and
deep

,
which
last
are
here
honoured

.
<EOS>
The

,

,
and

a

carried
through

.
<EOS>
This
is
a
Roman
work
,
and
the
more
I

,
appeared
the
more

.
<EOS>
A

who
lives
upon

,

entered
by
this

eight

morning
,
and
advanced

,
that

four

.
<EOS>
He

,

,

.
<EOS>
He

air

(

)

,

<EOS>
and

,

.
<EOS>
I

romanced

,

render

the
more

:
but
I

,
that
several

entered
this

,

a

by

,

,

(

)

eight

;
but

.
<EOS>
The

,
and

by

for

.
<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
But

<EOS>

du

<EOS>
It

<EOS>
The

,

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
Nice
was
originally
a
colony
from
Marseilles
.
<EOS>
You
know
the
Phocians
(
if
we
may
believe
Justin
and
Polybius
)
settled
in
Gaul
,
and
built
Marseilles
,
during
the
reign
of
Tarquinius
Priscus
at
Rome
.
<EOS>
This
city
flourished
to
such

degree
,
that
long
before
the

were
in
a
condition
to
extend
their
dominion
,

sent
forth

,
and
established

along
the
coast
of
Liguria
.
<EOS>
Of

,
Nice
,
or

,
was

of
the

;
so
called
,
in
all

,

,

,
in

some

obtained

,

.
<EOS>
Nice
,

,

in

,

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

;
and
nothing
is
more
common
,
than
to
see
the
most
execrable
villains
diverting
themselves
in
the
cloysters
of
some
convents
at
Rome
.
  
<EOS>
Nice
abounds
with
noblesse
,
marquisses
,
counts
,
and
barons
.
<EOS>
Of
these
,
three
or
four
families
are
really
respectable
:
the
rest
are
novi
homines
,
sprung
from
Bourgeois
,
who
have
saved
a
little
money
by
their
different
occupations
,
and
raised
themselves
to
the
rank
of
noblesse
by
purchase
.
<EOS>
One
is
descended
from
an
avocat
;
another
from
an
apothecary
;
a
third
from
a
retailer
of
wine
,
a
fourth
from
a
dealer
in
anchovies
;
and
I
am
told
,
there
is
actually
a
count
at
Villefranche
,
whose
father
sold
macaroni
in
the
streets
.
<EOS>
A
man
in
this
country
may
buy
a
marquisate
,
or
a
county
,
for
the
value
of
three
or
four
hundred
pounds
sterling
,
and
the
title
follows
the
fief
;
but
he
may
purchase
lettres
de
noblesse
for
about
thirty
or
forty
guineas
.
<EOS>
In
Savoy
,
there
are
six
hundred
families
of
noblesse
;
the
greater
part
of
which
have
not
above
one
hundred
crowns
a
year
to

.
<EOS>
In
the
mountains
of
Piedmont
,
and
even

this
country
of
Nice
,
there
are
some
representatives
of
very
antient

noble

,
reduced
to
the

of
common

;
but
they
still
retain
the
antient

of
their
houses
,
and
boast
of
the
noble
blood
that
runs
in

.
<EOS>
A
gentleman
told
me
,
that
in
travelling
through
the
mountains
,
he
was
obliged
to
pass
a
night
in
the

of
one

these

,
who
called

,
"
Chevalier
,
as
-
tu

?
"
<EOS>
"
Have

Hogs
,
Sir
Knight
?
<EOS>
"
This
,
however
,

not

case

the
noblesse
of
Nice
.
<EOS>
Two

three
of
them
have
about
four

five
hundred
a

:
the
rest
,

,
may

about

,

the

,

,

.
<EOS>
Some

are
well

,
commodious
,
and
situated
;
but
,
for
the
most

,

.
<EOS>
Our
noblesse
,
notwithstanding
their
origin
,
and
the
cheap
rate
at
which
their
titles
have
been

,
are

extremely

of
their

,
very

the

,
and
keep

Bourgeoisie
.
<EOS>
How
they
live
in
their
families
,
I
do
not
choose
to
enquire
;
but
,
in
public
,
Madame
appears
in
her

of

,
or

,
with
her

and

,

,

;
while
Monsieur
Le
Comte
struts
about
in
his
lace

.
<EOS>
Rouge
and
fard
are
more
peculiarly
necessary
in
this
country
,
where
the
complexion
and
skin
are
naturally
swarthy

.
<EOS>
I
have
likewise
observed
,
that
most

the
females
are
pot

;
a

owing
,
I
believe
,
to
the
great
quantity
of

which
they

.
<EOS>
All
the
horses
,
mules
,
asses
,
and
cattle
,
which
feed
upon
grass
,
have
the
same
distension
.
<EOS>
This
kind
of
food
produces
such
acid
juices
in
the
stomach
,
as
excite
a
perpetual
sense
of
hunger
.
<EOS>
I
have
been
often
amazed
at
the
voracious

of
these
people
.
<EOS>
You
must
not
expect
that
I
should
describe
the
tables
and
the

of
our
Nissard

.
<EOS>
Our
consul
,
who
is
a
very
honest
man
,
told
me
,
he
had
lived
four

thirty
years
in
the
country
,
without
having
once
eat
or

.
  
<EOS>
The
noblesse
of
Nice
can
not
leave
the
country
without
express

from
the
king
;
and
this

,
when
obtained
,
is
for
a
limited

,
which
they
dare

,
on
pain

his

's

.
<EOS>
They
must
,
therefore
,
endeavour
to
find
amusements
at
home
;
and
this
<EOS>
,
I
apprehend
,
would
be
no

for

active
spirit
or

.
<EOS>
True

is
,
the
religion
of
the
country
supplies
a
never

of

to
those
who
have
any
relish
for
devotion
;
and
this
is
here

prevailing

.
<EOS>
We
have
had
transient

of
a
puppet
-
shew
,
strolling

,
and
rope

;
but
they
did
not
like
their
quarters
,
and
decamped
without

.
<EOS>
In

summer
,
about
eight
or

,
part

the
noblesse
may
be

in
a

Pare
;

,

,
a
sort

,

,

,
which

a

sea
,
the

.
<EOS>
Here

stretched

,
like
so
many

the

by
moon

,
each

:
for

,
this

prevails
at
Nice
among

people
;
and

known
.
<EOS>
The

and
the
cicisbeo
live
together
as

;
and
the
wife
and
the
mistress
embrace

with

.
<EOS>
I
do
not
choose
to
enter
into
particulars
.
<EOS>
I
can
not
open
the
scandalous

of
Nice
,
without

.
<EOS>
With

,

peruse
dean
Swift
's

Yahoos
,
and
then

,

.
<EOS>
But

in

's

.
<EOS>
Just

one

our

,

in

the

,

,

.
<EOS>
Besides

,

the
commandant
's

,
where

.
<EOS>
In

,

,
at

,

,

.
<EOS>
At

,

:
but
,

,

,
and
if
Bourgeois
,

.
<EOS>
No

,

a

the

;
and

,
without

,

,
which

's

,
at

-
piece
,
and

.
<EOS>
If

have

to

,
I
can
not

more
than
a
couple

violins
;
and
,

,
it

called

.
  
<EOS>
Though

king
of
Sardinia
takes

to

of
Great
-
Britain
with
particular

respect
,
I

seen

,
that

;
and

,
and

,

.
<EOS>
For

,

;
and

,

fain
hope
<EOS>
have
hitherto

,
  
<EOS>
Dear
Sir
,
your
faithful
,

.
   
<EOS>
NICE
,
September
2
,
1764
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
DOCTOR
,
 
I
wrote
in
May
to
Mr
B
  
at
Geneva
,
and
gave
him
what
information
he
desired
to
have
,
touching
the
conveniences
of
Nice
.
<EOS>
I
shall
now
enter
into
the
same
detail
,
for
the
benefit
of
such
of
your
friends
or
patients
,
as
may
have
occasion
to
try
this
climate
.
  
<EOS>
The
journey
from
Calais
to
Nice
,
of
four
persons
in
a
coach
,
or
two
post
-

,
with
a
servant
on

,
travelling
post
,
may
be
performed
with
ease
,
for
about
one
hundred
and
twenty

,
including
every

.
<EOS>
Either
at
Calais
or
at
Paris
,
you
will
always
find
a
travelling
coach
or
berline
,
which
you
may
buy
for
thirty
or
forty
guineas
,
and
this
will
serve
very
well
to
reconvey
you
to
your
own
country
.
  
<EOS>
In
the
town
of
Nice
,
you
will
find
no
ready
-

for
a
whole
family
.
<EOS>
Just
without
one
of
the
gates
,
there
are
two
houses
to
be
let
,
ready
-
furnished
,
for
about
five
loui'dores
per
month
.
<EOS>
As
for
the
country
houses
in
this
neighbourhood
,
they
are
damp
in
winter
,
and
generally
without
chimnies
;
and
in
summer
they
are
rendered
uninhabitable
by
the
heat
and
the
vermin
.
<EOS>
If
you
hire
a
tenement
in
Nice
,
you
must
take
it
for
a
year
certain
;
and
this
will
cost
you
about
twenty
pounds
sterling
.
<EOS>
For
this
price
,
I
have
a
ground
floor
paved
with
brick
,
consisting
of
a
kitchen
,
two
large
halls
,
a
couple
of
good
rooms
with
chimnies
,
three
large
closets
that
serve
for
bed
-
chambers
,
and
dressing
-
rooms
,
a
butler
's
room
,
and
three
apartments
for
servants
,
lumber
or
stores
,
to
which
we
ascend
by
narrow

stairs
.
<EOS>
I
have
likewise
two
small
gardens
,
well
stocked
with
oranges
,
lemons
,
peaches
,
figs
,
grapes
,
corinths
,
sallad
,
and
pot
-

.
<EOS>
It
is
supplied
with
a
draw
-
well
of
good
water
,
and
there
is
another
in
the
vestibule
of
the
house
,
which
is
cool
,
large
,
and
magnificent
.
<EOS>
You
may
hire
furniture
for
such

tenement
for
about
two
guineas
a
month
:
but
I
chose
rather
to
buy
what
was
necessary
;
and
this
cost
me
about
sixty
pounds
.
<EOS>
I
suppose
it
will
fetch
me
about
half
the
money
when
I
leave
the
place
.
<EOS>
It
is
very
difficult
to
find
a
tolerable
cook
at
Nice
.
<EOS>
A
common
maid
,
who
serves
the
people
of
the
country
,
for
three
or
four
livres
a
month
,
will
not
live
with
an
English
family
under
eight
or
ten
.
<EOS>
They
are
all
slovenly
,
slothful
,
and
unconscionable
cheats
.
<EOS>
The
markets
at
Nice
are
tolerably
well
supplied
.
<EOS>
Their
beef
,
which
comes
from
Piedmont
,
is
pretty
good
,
and
we
have
it
all
the
year
.
<EOS>
In
the
winter
we
have
likewise
excellent
pork
,
and
delicate
lamb
;
but
the
mutton
is
indifferent
.
<EOS>
Piedmont
,
also
,
affords
us
delicious
capons
,
fed
with
maize
;
and
this
country
produces
excellent
turkeys
,
but
very
few
geese
.
<EOS>
Chickens
and
pullets
are
extremely
meagre
.
<EOS>
I
have
tried
to
fatten
them
,
without
success
.
<EOS>
In
summer
they
are
subject
to
the
pip
,
and
die
in
great
numbers
.
<EOS>
Autumn
and
winter
are
the
seasons
for
game
;
hares
,
partridges
,
quails
,
wild
-
pigeons
,
woodcocks
,
snipes
,
thrushes
,
beccaficas
,
and
ortolans
.
<EOS>
Wild
-
boar
is
sometimes
found
in
the
mountains
:
it
has
a
delicious
taste
,
not
unlike
that
of
the
wild
hog
in
Jamaica
;
and
would
make
an
excellent
barbecue
,
about
the
beginning
of
winter
,
when
it
is
in
good
case
:
<EOS>
but
,
when
meagre
,
the
head
only
is
presented
at
tables
.
<EOS>
Pheasants
are
very
scarce
.
<EOS>
As
for
the
heath
-
game
,
I
never
saw
but
one
cock
,
which
my
servant
bought
in
the
market
,
and
brought
home
;
but
the
commandant
's
cook
came
into
my
kitchen
,
and
carried
it
of
,
after
it
was
half
plucked
,
saying
,
his
master
had
company
to
dinner
.
<EOS>
The
hares
are
large
,
plump
,
and
juicy
.
<EOS>
The
partridges
are
generally
of
the
red
sort
;
large
as
pullets
,
and
of
a
good
flavour
:
there
are
also
some
grey
partridges
in
the
mountains
;
and
another
sort
of
a
white
colour
,
that
weigh
four
or
five
pounds
each
.
<EOS>
Beccaficas
are
smaller
than
sparrows
,
but
very
fat
,
and
they
are
generally
eaten
half
raw
.
<EOS>
The
best
way
of
dressing
them
is
to
stuff
them
into
a
roll
,
scooped
of
<EOS>
it
's
crum
;
to
baste
them
well
with
butter
,
and
roast
them
,
until
they
are
brown
and
crisp
.
<EOS>
The
ortolans
are
kept
in
cages
,
and
crammed
,
until
they
die
of
fat
,
then
eaten
as
dainties
.
<EOS>
The
thrush
is
presented
with
the
trail
,
because
the
bird
feeds
on
olives
.
<EOS>
They
may
as
well
eat
the
trail
of
a
sheep
,
because
it
feeds
on
the
aromatic
herbs
of
the
mountain
.
<EOS>
In
the
summer
,
we
have
beef
,
veal
,
and
mutton
,
chicken
,
and
ducks
;
which
last
are
very
fat
,
and
very
flabby
.
<EOS>
All
the
meat
is
tough
in
this
season
,
because
the
excessive
heat
,
and
great
number
of
flies
,
will
not
admit
of
its
being
kept
any
time
after
it
is
killed
.
<EOS>
Butter
and
milk
,
though
not
very
delicate
,
we
have
all
the
year
.
<EOS>
Our
tea
and
fine
sugar
come
from
Marseilles
,
at
a
very
reasonable
price
.
  
<EOS>
Nice
is
not
without
variety
of
fish
;
though
they
are
not
counted
so
good
in
their
kinds
as
those
of
the
ocean
.
<EOS>
Soals
,
and
flat
-
fish
in
general
,
are
scarce
.
<EOS>
Here
are
some
mullets
,
both
grey
and
red
.
<EOS>
We
sometimes
see
the
dory
,
which
is
called
St
Pierre
;
with
rock
-
fish
,
bonita
,
and
mackarel
.
<EOS>
The
gurnard
appears
pretty
often
;
and
there
is
plenty
of
a
kind
of
large
whiting
,
which
eats
pretty
well
;
but
has
not
the
delicacy
of
that
which
is
caught
on
our
coast
.
<EOS>
One
of
the
best
fish
of
this
country
,
is
called
Le
Loup
,
about
two
or
three
pounds
in
weight
;
white
,
firm
,
and
well
-
flavoured
.
<EOS>
Another
,
no
-
way
inferior
to
it
,
is
the
Moustel
,
about
the
same
size
;
of
a
dark
-
grey
colour
,
and
short
,
blunt
snout
;
growing
thinner
and
flatter
from
the
shoulders
downwards
,
so
as
to
resemble
a
soal
at
the
tail
.
<EOS>
This
can
not
be
the
mustela
of
the
antients
,
which
is
supposed
to
be
the
sea
lamprey
.
<EOS>
Here
too
are
found
the
vyvre
,
or
,
as
we
call
it
,
weaver
;
remarkable
for
its
long
,
sharp
spines
,
so
dangerous
to
the
fingers
of
the
fishermen
.
<EOS>
We
have
abundance
of
the
saepia
,
or
cuttle
-
fish
,
of
which
the
people
in
this
country
make
a
delicate
ragout
;
as
also
of
the
polype
de
mer
,
which
is
an
ugly
animal
,
with
long
feelers
,
like
tails
,
which
they
often
wind
about
the
legs
of
the
fishermen
.
<EOS>
They
are
stewed
with
onions
,
and
eat
something
like
cow
-
heel
.
<EOS>
The
market
sometimes
affords
the
ecrivisse
de
mer
,
which
is
a
lobster
without
claws
,
of
a
sweetish
taste
;
and
there
are
a
few
rock
oysters
,
very
small
and
very
rank
.
<EOS>
Sometimes
the
fishermen
find
under
water
,
pieces
of
a
very
hard
cement
,
like
plaister
of
Paris
,
which
contain
a
kind
of
muscle
,
called
la
datte
,
from
its
resemblance
to
a
date
.
<EOS>
These
petrifactions
are
commonly
of
a
triangular
form
and
may
weigh
about
twelve
or
fifteen
pounds
each
and
one
of
them
may
contain
a
dozen
of
these
muscles
which
have
nothing
extraordinary
in
the
taste
or
flavour
,
though
extremely
curious
,
as
found
alive
and
juicy
,
in
the
heart
of
a
rock
,
almost
as
hard
as
marble
,
without
any
visible
communication
with
the
air
or
water
.
<EOS>
I
take
it
for
granted
,
however
,
that
the
inclosing
cement
is
porous
,
and
admits
the
finer
parts
of
the
surrounding
fluid
.
<EOS>
In
order
to
reach
the
muscles
,
this
cement
must
be
broke
with
large
hammers
;
and
it
may
be
truly
said
,
the
kernal
is
not
worth
the
trouble
of
cracking
the
shell
.
<EOS>
[
These
are
found
in
great
plenty
at
Ancona
and
other
parts
of
the
Adriatic
,
where
they
go
by
the
name
of
Bollani
,
as
we
are
informed
by
Keysler
.
]
<EOS>
Among
the
fish
of
this
country
,
there
is
a
very
ugly
animal
of
the
eel
species
,
which
might
pass
for
a
serpent
:
it
is
of
a
dusky
,
black
colour
,
marked
with
spots
of
yellow
,
about
eighteen
inches
,
or
two
feet
long
.
<EOS>
The
Italians
call
it
murena
;
but
whether
it
is
the
fish
which
had
the
same
name
among
the
antient
Romans
,
I
can
not
pretend
to
determine
.
<EOS>
The
antient
murena
was
counted
a
great
delicacy
,
and
was
kept
in
ponds
for
extraordinary
occasions
.
<EOS>
Julius
Caesar
borrowed
six
thousand
for
one
entertainment
:
but
I
imagined
this
was
the
river
lamprey
.
<EOS>
The
murena
of
this
country
is
in
no
esteem
,
and
only
eaten
by
the
poor
people
.
  
<EOS>
Craw
-
fish
and
trout
are
rarely
found
in
the
rivers
among
the
mountains
.
<EOS>
The
sword
-
fish
is
much
esteemed
in
Nice
,
and
called
l'empereur
,
about
six
or
seven
feet
long
:
but
I
have
never
seen
it
.
<EOS>
[
Since
I
wrote
the
above
letter
,
I
have
eaten
several
times
of
this
fish
,
which
is
as
white
as
the
finest
veal
,
and
extremely
delicate
.
<EOS>
The
emperor
associates
with
the
tunny
fish
,
and
is
always
taken
in
their
company
.
]
<EOS>
They
are
very
scarce
;
and
when
taken
,
are
generally
concealed
,
because
the
head
belongs
to
the
commandant
,
who
has
likewise
the
privilege
of
buying
the
best
fish
at
a
very
low
price
.
<EOS>
For
which
reason
,
the
choice
pieces
are
concealed
by
the
fishermen
,
and
sent
privately
to
Piedmont
or
Genoa
.
<EOS>
But
,
the
chief
fisheries
on
this
coast
are
of
the
sardines
,
anchovies
,
and
tunny
.
<EOS>
These
are
taken
in
small
quantities
all
the
year
;
but
spring
and
summer
is
the
season
when
they
mostly
abound
.
<EOS>
In
June
and
July
,
a
fleet
of
about
fifty
fishing
-
boats
puts
to
sea
every
evening
about
eight
o'clock
,
and
catches
anchovies
in
immense
quantities
.
<EOS>
One
small
boat
sometimes
takes
in
one
night
twenty
-
five
rup
,
amounting
to
six
hundred
weight
;
but
it
must
be
observed
,
that
the
pound
here
,
as
well
as
in
other
parts
of
Italy
,
consists
but
of
twelve
ounces
.
<EOS>
Anchovies
,
besides
their
making
a
considerable
article
in
the
commerce
of
Nice
,
are
a
great
resource
in
all
families
.
<EOS>
The
noblesse
and
burgeois
sup
on
sallad
and
anchovies
,
which
are
eaten
on
all
their
meagre
days
.
<EOS>
The
fishermen
and
mariners
all
along
this
coast
have
scarce
any
other
food
but
dry
bread
,
with
a
few
pickled
anchovies
;
and
when
the
fish
is
eaten
,
they
rub
their
crusts
with
the
brine
.
<EOS>
Nothing
can
be
more
delicious
than
fresh
anchovies
fried
in
oil
:
I
prefer
them
to
the
smelts
of
the
Thames
.
<EOS>
I
need
not
mention
,
that
the
sardines
and
anchovies
are
caught
in
nets
;
salted
,
barrelled
,
and
exported
into
all
the
different
kingdoms
and
states
of
Europe
.
<EOS>
The
sardines
,
however
,
are
largest
and
fattest
in
the
month
of
September
.
<EOS>
A
company
of
adventurers
have
farmed
the
tunny
-
fishery
of
the
king
,
for
six
years
;
a
monopoly
,
for
which
they
pay
about
three
thousand
pounds
sterling
.
<EOS>
They
are
at
a
very
considerable
expence
for
nets
,
boats
,
and
attendance
.
<EOS>
Their
nets
are
disposed
in
a
very
curious
manner
across
the
small
bay
of
St
Hospice
,
in
this
neighbourhood
,
where
the
fish
chiefly
resort
.
<EOS>
They
are
never
removed
,
except
in
the
winter
,
and
when
they
want
repair
:
but
there
are
avenues
for
the
fish
to
enter
,
and
pass
,
from
one
inclosure
to
another
.
<EOS>
There
is
a
man
in
a
boat
,
who
constantly
keeps
watch
.
<EOS>
When
he
perceives
they
are
fairly
entered
,
he
has
a
method
for
shutting
all
the
passes
,
and
confining
the
fish
to
one
apartment
of
the
net
,
which
is
lifted
up
into
the
boat
,
until
the
prisoners
are
taken
and
secured
.
<EOS>
The
tunny
-
fish
generally
runs
from
fifty
to
one
hundred
weight
;
but
some
of
them
are
much
larger
.
<EOS>
They
are
immediately
gutted
,
boiled
,
and
cut
in
slices
.
<EOS>
The
guts
and
head
afford
oil
:
the
slices
are
partly
dried
,
to
be
eaten
occasionally
with
oil
and
vinegar
,
or
barrelled
up
in
oil
,
to
be
exported
.
<EOS>
It
is
counted
a
delicacy
in
Italy
and
Piedmont
,
and
tastes
not
unlike
sturgeon
.
<EOS>
The
famous
pickle
of
the
ancients
,
called
garum
,
was
made
of
the
gills
and
blood
of
the
tunny
,
or
thynnus
.
<EOS>
There
is
a
much
more
considerable
fishery
of
it
in
Sardinia
,
where
it
is
said
to
employ
four
hundred
persons
;
but
this
belongs
to
the
duc
de
St
Pierre
.
<EOS>
In
the
neighbourhood
of
Villa
Franca
,
there
are
people
always
employed
in
fishing
for
coral
and
sponge
,
which
grow
adhering
to
the
rocks
under
water
.
<EOS>
Their
methods
do
not
favour
much
of
ingenuity
.
<EOS>
For
the
coral
,
they
lower
down
a
swab
,
composed
of
what
is
called
spunyarn
on
board
our
ships
of
war
,
hanging
in
distinct
threads
,
and
sunk
by
means
of
a
great
weight
,
which
,
striking
against
the
coral
in
its
descent
,
disengages
it
from
the
rocks
;
and
some
of
the
pieces
being
intangled
among
the
threads
of
the
swab
,
are
brought
up
with
it
above
water
.
<EOS>
The
sponge
is
got
by
means
of
a
cross
-
stick
,
fitted
with
hooks
,
which
being
lowered
down
,
fastens
upon
it
,
and
tears
it
from
the
rocks
.
<EOS>
In
some
parts
of
the
Adriatic
and
Archipelago
,
these
substances
are
gathered
by
divers
,
who
can
remain
five
minutes
below
water
.
<EOS>
But
I
will
not
detain
you
one
minute
longer
;
though
I
must
observe
,
that
there
is
plenty
of
fine
samphire
growing
along
all
these
rocks
,
neglected
and
unknown
.
 
<EOS>
Adieu
.
   
<EOS>
NICE
,
October
10
,
1764
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
Before
I
tell
you
the
price
of
provisions
at
Nice
,
it
will
be
necessary
to
say
something
of
the
money
.
<EOS>
The
gold
coin
of
Sardinia
consists
of
the
doppia
di
savoia
,
value
twenty
-
four
livres
Piedmontese
,
about
the
size
of
a
loui'dore
;
and
the
mezzo
doppia
,
or
piece
of
twelve
livres
.
<EOS>
In
silver
,
there
is
the
scudo
of
six
livres
,
the
mezzo
scudo
of
three
;
and
the
quarto
,
or
pezza
di
trenta
soldi
:
but
all
these
are
very
scarce
.
<EOS>
We
seldom
see
any
gold
and
silver
coin
,
but
the
loui'dore
,
and
the
six
,
and
three
-
livre
Pieces
of
France
;
a
sure
sign
that
the
French
suffer
by
their
contraband
commerce
with
the
Nissards
.
<EOS>
The
coin
chiefly
used
at
market
is
a
piece
of
copper
silvered
,
that
passes
for
seven
sols
and
a
half
;
another
of
the
same
sort
,
valued
two
sols
and
a
half
.
<EOS>
They
have
on
one
side
the
impression
of
the
king
's
head
;
and
on
the
other
,
the
arms
of
Savoy
,
with
a
ducal
crown
,
inscribed
with
his
name
and
titles
.
<EOS>
There
are
of
genuine
copper
,
pieces
of
one
sol
,
stamped
on
one
side
with
a
cross
fleuree
;
and
on
the
reverse
,
with
the
king
's
cypher
and
crown
,
inscribed
as
the
others
:
finally
,
there
is
another
small
copper
piece
,
called
piccalon
,
the
sixth
part
of
a
sol
,
with
a
plain
cross
,
and
on
the
reverse
,
a
slip
-
knot
surmounted
with
a
crown
;
the
legend
as
above
.
<EOS>
The
impression
and
legend
on
the
gold
and
silver
coins
,
are
the
same
as
those
on
the
pieces
of
seven
sols
and
a
half
.
<EOS>
The
livre
of
Piedmont
consists
of
twenty
sols
,
and
is
very
near
of
the
same
value
as
an
English
shilling
:
<EOS>
ten
sols
,
therefore
,
are
equal
to
six
-
pence
sterling
.
<EOS>
Butcher
's
meat
in
general
sells
at
Nice
for
three
sols
a
pound
;
and
veal
is
something
dearer
:
<EOS>
but
then
there
are
but
twelve
ounces
in
the
pound
,
which
being
allowed
for
,
sixteen
ounces
<EOS>
,
come
for
something
less
than
twopence
halfpenny
English
.
<EOS>
Fish
commonly
sells
for
four
sols
the
twelve
ounces
,
or
five
for
the
English
pound
;
and
these
five
are
equivalent
to
three
-
pence
of
our
money
:
but
sometimes
we
are
obliged
to
pay
five
,
and
even
six
sols
for
the
Piedmontese
pound
of
fish
.
<EOS>
A
turkey
that
would
sell
for
five
or
six
shillings
at
the
London
market
,
costs
me
but
three
at
Nice
.
<EOS>
I
can
buy
a
good
capon
for
thirty
sols
,
or
eighteen
-
pence
;
and
the
same
price
I
pay
for
a
brace
of
partridges
,
or
a
good
hare
.
<EOS>
I
can
have
a
woodcock
for
twenty
-
four
sols
;
but
the
pigeons
are
dearer
than
in
London
.
<EOS>
Rabbits
are
very
rare
;
and
there
is
scarce
a
goose
to
be
seen
in
the
whole
county
of
Nice
.
<EOS>
Wild
-
ducks
and
teal
are
sometimes
to
be
had
in
the
winter
;
and
now
I
am
speaking
of
sea
-
fowl
,
it
may
not
be
amiss
to
tell
you
what
I
know
of
the
halcyon
,
or
king's
-
fisher
.
<EOS>
It
is
a
bird
,
though
very
rare
in
this
country
about
the
size
of
a
pigeon
;
the
body
brown
,
and
the
belly
white
:
<EOS>
by
a
wonderful
instinct
it
makes
its
nest
upon
the
surface
of
the
sea
,
and
lays
its
eggs
in
the
month
of
November
,
when
the
Mediterranean
is
always
calm
and
smooth
as
a
mill
-
pond
.
<EOS>
The
people
about
here
call
them
martinets
,
because
they
begin
to
hatch
about
Martinmass
.
<EOS>
Their

conceptivae
,
their

and

;
their

,
or

,
and
their
precidaneae
,

.
<EOS>
The

were

in
January
;
the

,

;

,

;

;

,

;
and

.
<EOS>
They

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Besides

,

.
<EOS>
Every

,

.
<EOS>
Every

was

,
thence
called

.
<EOS>
There

,

;

,

,

et

,

Allia
in

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

,

<EOS>
This

;
and
the

must
have

.
<EOS>
I

<EOS>
Instances

<EOS>
But

Your

NICE
,

.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
DOCTOR
,
 
In

about
the

of
Nice
,
I

to
trust
to
the
information
of
the
inhabitants
,
who
are
much

.
<EOS>
They

,

of
this

amount

one

livres
,
or
five
thousand
pounds
sterling
;
of

I
would
strike

at

one
fourth
,
as
an

of
their
own
vanity
:
<EOS>
perhaps
,

we
deduct
a
third
,
it
will
be
nearer
the
truth
.
<EOS>
For
,
I

not
find

they

,
but
the

and
the

,

at

to
the
best

;
and

,

;
but

.
<EOS>
The

draw
from
Nice
one

,

-

,

,
in

,

;
an

;

.
<EOS>
These

<EOS>

.
<EOS>
The

;

;

,

,

.
<EOS>
In

,

,

,

<EOS>
[

]
<EOS>
The

,

,

,

<EOS>
The

,

,

,

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
And

,

,

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>
If

<EOS>
It

<EOS>
In

<EOS>
They

<EOS>
They

<EOS>
You

;

,

,

<EOS>
I

men
of

<EOS>
this

;
but

,

.
<EOS>
It

,

,

.
<EOS>
Here

:

.
<EOS>
They

,

.
<EOS>
There

.
<EOS>
Though

,

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
They

.
<EOS>
Among

,

;

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

condition

;
or
,

,

;
for

;
and

.
<EOS>
Their

is
what
they

Patois
;
though

,

.
 
<EOS>
Patois
,
from

Latin

,
means

,
or

.
<EOS>
It

Patavium
,

,
which

of
Livy
,

,

,

.
<EOS>
The
Patois
,
or
native

of
Nice
,
is
no
other
than
the
ancient
Provencal
,
from
which
the
Italian
,
Spanish
and

,

.
<EOS>
This
is
the
language
that
rose
upon
the

of
the
Latin

,
after
the

of
the
Goths
,

,

,

,
by

.
<EOS>
It

spoke
all

,
Spain
,

,
until
the

,
when
the

began
to

into
the

which

now
call

own
:
The
Spaniards
and
French
,
likewise
,

it
into
their

.
<EOS>
From
its
great
affinity
to
the
Latin
,
it
was

Romance
,
a

which
the
Spaniards
still
give

.
<EOS>
As

first

of

-

were

in

,
all

of
the
same

,
have
derived
from
it
the
name
of
romance
;
and
as
those
annals
of
chivalry
contained
extravagant

of
knights
,
giants
,
and
necromancers
,
<EOS>
every
improbable
story
or
fiction
is
to
this
day
called
a
romance
.
<EOS>
Mr
Walpole
,
in
his
Catalogue
of
royal
and
noble
Authors
,
has

in
the
antient
Provencal
,

by

king
Richard
I
surnamed

de

;
and
Voltaire
,
in
his
Historical

,
has

.
<EOS>
The
Patois
of
Nice
,
must
,
without
doubt
,
have
undergone
changes
and

in
the
course
of
so
many
ages
,
especially
as
no

,
either

or

.
<EOS>
It
is
neglected
,
as
the
language
of
the
vulgar
:
and
scarce
any
-
body
here
knows
either
its
origin
or

.
<EOS>
I
have
in

Provencal
,
that
I

Patois
:
but
I

no

to
give

least

.
<EOS>
The

of

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Almost

the
Patois
may

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Cavallo
,
signifying

in

;

,

,
is
changed
into

;
aqua
,
which
means

,

call

.
<EOS>
To
express
,
what

slop
is
here
!
<EOS>
they
say
acco
fa
lac
aqui

<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
I
had
once
thoughts
of
writing
a
complete

of
this
town
and
county
:
<EOS>
but
I
found
myself
altogether
unequal
to
the

.
<EOS>
I
have
neither
health
,
strength
,
nor
opportunity
to
make
proper

of
the

,

,
and

.
<EOS>
I

not

with
these

natural

.
<EOS>
I
have
no
books
to
direct

.
<EOS>
I
can
find
no

least

;
and
I

they

to
many

,

;
and

,

before
,

by

.
<EOS>
You

with
such
imperfect

as
my

can

.
  
<EOS>
The

at
Nice
,

these
,
gardening

,

their

,

,

,

;

the

;

,

.
  
<EOS>
Nothing

,

.
<EOS>
In

,

,

.
<EOS>
They

no

.
<EOS>
The

,

,
is

very

;
and

.
<EOS>
They

'

,

.
<EOS>
Every

opens
,

,

of
office

the

;

Nice
,
every

,

.
<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

and
choosing
a
situation
among
the
heath
,
begins
to
spin
in
a
most
curious

,
until

quite
inclosed
,
  
and
the
cocon
or

of

,
about
the
size
of
a

's

,
which
he

produced
remains

.
<EOS>
It
is
no
unusual

see

cocons
,
spun
by
two
worms
included

.
<EOS>
There

of

to

.
<EOS>
One

,
four
rup
,

Nice
pounds

;
and
one

,

of

,

;
that

,

of

one

,
which

its

,

.
<EOS>
In

;

.
<EOS>
In

.
<EOS>
The

,

<EOS>
The

of

,

in

,

.
<EOS>
It

,

,

<EOS>
The

,

.
<EOS>
Before

<EOS>
A

<EOS>
Six

,

<EOS>
There

Nice
,

<EOS>
Those

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
Hard

mill

,

<EOS>
There

Nice
,

,

<EOS>
In

Nice
 
<EOS>

<EOS>
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
In
my
last
,
I
gave

a
succinct

of
the
silkworm
,
and
the
management
of
that
curious

in
this
country
.
<EOS>
I
shall
now
proceed
to
describe
the
methods
of
making
wine
and
oil
.
  
<EOS>
The
vintage
begins
in
September
.
<EOS>
The
grapes
being
chosen
and
carefully
picked
,
are
put
into
a
large
vat
,
where
they
are
pressed
by
a
man
's
naked
feet
,
and
the
juices
drawn
off
by
a
cock
below
.
<EOS>
When
no

is
procured
by
this
operation
,
the
bruised
grapes
are
put
into
the
press
,
and
yield
still
more
liquor
.
<EOS>
The
juice
obtained
by
this
double
pressure
,
being
put
in
casks
,
with
their
bungs
open
,
begins
to
ferment
and
discharge
its
impurities
at

.
<EOS>
The
waste
occasioned
by
this
discharge
,
is
constantly
supplied
with
fresh
wine
,
so
that
the
casks
are
always
full
.
<EOS>
The
fermentation
continues
for
twelve
,
fifteen
,
or
twenty
days
,
according
to
the
strength
and
vigour
of
the

.
<EOS>
In
about
a
month
,
the
wine
is
fit
for

.
<EOS>
When
the
grapes
are
of
a
bad
,
meagre

,
the

mix
the
juice
with
pigeons'-dung
or
quick
-

,
in
order
to
give
it
a
spirit
which
nature
has
denied
:
but
this
is
a
very
mischievous
adulteration
.
  
<EOS>
The
process
for
oil
-
making
is
equally
simple
.
<EOS>
The
best
olives
are
those
that
grow
wild
;
but
the
quantity
of
them
is
very
inconsiderable
.
<EOS>
Olives
begin
to
ripen
and
drop
in
the
beginning
of
November
:
but
some
remain
on
the
trees
till
February
,
and
even

April
,
and
these
are
counted
the
most
valuable
.
<EOS>
When
the
olives
are
gathered
,
they

manufactured

,
before
they
fade
and
grow
wrinkled
,
otherwise
they

produce
bad

.
<EOS>
They
are
first
of
all
ground
into
a

by
a
mill
-
stone
set
edge
-
ways
in
a
circular
stone
-
trough
,
the
wheel
being
turned
by
water
.
  
<EOS>
This

is
put
into

or
circular
cases
made
of
grass

,
having
a
round

at

;
when

our

.
<EOS>
A

,

in

press
,
and
being

,
the
oil
with
all

,
runs

below

in

ground
.
<EOS>
From

is
laded
into
a

,
half
filled

water
.
<EOS>
The
sordes
or

falls
to
the
bottom
;
the

swims
a
-
top
;
and

.
<EOS>
What

vat
,
is

into
a
large
stone
cistern
with
water
,
and
after

,
and
standing
twelve
or

days
,
yields
a
coarser
oil
used
for

and

.
<EOS>
After

,

extract
an

still

and

from
the

.
<EOS>
Sometimes
,

,

,

:
but

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

,
and
sold
at
a
very

,
because

.
<EOS>
Even

,

is
sold

and
used

.
  
<EOS>
I

now

of
Nice
which

.
<EOS>
True

,

some
coarse
paper
made
in
this

;

:
but

:

<EOS>
Carpenter
's

.
<EOS>
There

,

<EOS>
Nothing

,

,

.
<EOS>
All

in

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

,

.
  
<EOS>
The

,

;

,

.
<EOS>
They

,
and

.
<EOS>
The

of

better

,

,
the

,

.
<EOS>
People

with

,

,
painted

,
tapestry
,

.
<EOS>
All

doors
,
as
well
as
the
windows
,

of
folding

.
<EOS>
As

no

in
the
rooms
,
which
are
divided
by

and
the

,
fires
are
of
much
less

.
<EOS>
Wainscot
would
afford
harbour
for
bugs
:
besides
,
white

have
a
better
effect
in
this

.
<EOS>
The

commonly
used
in

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Instead

,

;

.
  
<EOS>
In

,

;
and

in

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

is

.
<EOS>
I
think
there

two

in
Nice
,
besides
that

the
commandant
:
but
there

sedan

,
which

.
<EOS>
When

in

summer
,
I
paid

,

-
pence
,

,

.
  
<EOS>
Now

,

,

,

ought

,

,

very

's

.
<EOS>
The

<EOS>
May.

;

<EOS>
But

,

.
<EOS>
There

,

,
no

,

the

sex

,

;
for
the

,

.
<EOS>
If

;

,

.
<EOS>
All

<EOS>
What

<EOS>

<EOS>
The
constitution
of
this
climate
may
be
pretty

,

inclosed

,

.
<EOS>
From

the
care
and
precautions
we
can
take
,
we
are
pestered
with
incredible
swarms
of
flies
,
fleas
,
and
bugs
;
but
the
gnats
,
or
couzins
,
are
more
intolerable
than
all
the
rest
.
<EOS>
In
the
day
-
time
,
it
is
impossible
to
keep
the
flies
out
of
your
mouth
,
nostrils
,
eyes
,
and
ears
.
<EOS>
They
croud
into
your
milk
,
tea
,
chocolate
,
soup
,
wine
,
and
water
:
they
soil
your
sugar
,
contaminate
your
victuals
,
and
devour
your
fruit
;
they
cover
and
defile
your
furniture
,
floors
,
cielings
,
and
indeed
your
whole

.
<EOS>
As
soon

are
lighted
,
the
couzins
begin
to
buz
about
your
ears
in
myriads
,
and
torment
you
with
their
stings
,
so
that
you
have
no
rest
nor
respite
 
'
till
you
get
into
bed
,
where
you
are
secured
by
your
mosquito
-
net
.
<EOS>
This
inclosure
is
very
disagreeable
in
hot
weather
;
and
very
inconvenient
to
those
,
who
,
like
me
,
are
subject
to
a
cough
and
spitting
.
<EOS>
It
is
moreover
ineffectual
;
for
some

those
cursed

insinuate
themselves
within
it
,
almost
every

;
and
half

are
sufficient
to
disturb

'
till
morning
.
<EOS>
This
is
a
plague
that
continues
all

year
;
but
in
summer
it
is
intolerable
.
<EOS>
During
this
season
,
likewise
,
the
moths
are
so

,
that

requires
the

to

.
<EOS>
From

month
of
May
,
 
'
till
the

October
,
the
heat
is
so

,
that
you
can
not

after

the
morning
 
'
till
eight

night
,
so
that

are
entirely

the
benefit

exercise
:
<EOS>
There
is
no

in
,
or
near
the
town
;
and
there
is
neither
coach
nor
chaise
to

,

.
<EOS>
Indeed
,

no
road
fit
for
any
wheel

,
but
the
common

to
the
Var
,
in

are

of
the
sun
from
the
sand
and
stones
,
and
<EOS>
at

same

half

with

.
<EOS>
If

ride
out
in
the
cool
of
the
evening
,
you
will
have
the
disadvantage
of
returning
in

dark
.
  
<EOS>
Among
the
demerits
of
Nice
,
I
must
also
mention
the
water
which
is
used
in

city
.
<EOS>
It
is
drawn
from
wells
;
and
for
the
most
part
so
hard
,
that

curdles
with
soap
.
<EOS>
There
are
many

and

in
the
neighbourhood
,
that

,
which
,

,

,

:
but

.
<EOS>
[
General
Paterson

Plan

with

;
but

.
]
<EOS>
I
have
a
draw
-
well
in

,

,
which
supply

for

;
but

,

.
<EOS>
Our

;
and
when

,
in
the
brook

Limpia
,

.
  
<EOS>
In

of

,

ought

of
Rocabiliare
,

,

.
<EOS>
There

,

;

<EOS>
I

,
which

Rocabiliare
,

's

.
<EOS>
He

much

;

is

,

.
<EOS>
They

,

,

<EOS>
The

,
lying

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
The

at

:
there

no

,

;
and

,

.
<EOS>
If

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
This

Nice
,

,

,
where

.
<EOS>
Last

<EOS>

<EOS>
He

me
<EOS>
,

,

,

<EOS>

<EOS>

,

<EOS>
NICE
,

1765
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
It
was
in
deference
to
your
opinion
,
reinforced
by
my
own
inclination
,
and
the
repeated
advice
of
other
friends
,
that
I
resolved
upon
my
late

to
Italy
.
<EOS>
I
could
plainly
perceive
from
the
anxious
solicitude
,
and
pressing

contained
in
all
the
letters
I
had
lately
received
from
my
correspondents
in
Britain
,
that
you
had
all
despaired
of
my
recovery
.
<EOS>
You
advised
me
to
make
a
pilgrimage
among
the
Alps
,
and
the
advice
was
good
.
<EOS>
In
scrambling
among
those
mountains
,
I
should
have
benefited
by
the
exercise
,
and
at
the
same
time
have
breathed
a
cool
,
pure
,
salubrious
air
,
which
,
in
all
probability
,
would
have
expelled
the
slow
fever
arising
in
a
great
measure
from
the

of
this
climate
.
<EOS>
But
,
I
wanted
a
companion
and
fellow
traveller
,
whose
conversation
and
society
could
alleviate
the
horrors
of
solitude
.
<EOS>
Besides
,
I
was
not
strong
enough
to
encounter
the
want
of
conveniences
,
and
even
of
necessaries
to
which
I
must
have
been
exposed
in

such

.
<EOS>
My
worthy
friend
Dr
A
  
earnestly

to
try
the
effect
of
a
sea
-
voyage
,
which
you
know
has
been
found
of

in

.
<EOS>
After
some

,
I
resolved

,
which
I

now
happily

.
<EOS>
I
had
a
most
eager

to

Florence

:
I
longed

,

,
and

,
which
I

so

.
<EOS>
I
felt
an

to

that
very
classical

which
had
been
the
scene
of
so
many
great
atchievements
;
and
I
could
not
bear
the

of
returning
to
England
from
the
very

of
Italy
,
without

.
<EOS>
With

,
I

as
to
enjoy
all
the

,
a

,

.
  
<EOS>
Rome
is
betwixt
four

five

from
Nice
,
and
one

the
way
I

.
<EOS>
Indeed

from

Genoa
,

,
and

,

.
<EOS>
The

,
which
are
no

,

,

Genoa
;
and

,

,
<EOS>
consequently

carried

face

the

,
except

certain

,
which

.
<EOS>
But
,

road

,

,

,

:

,

,

<EOS>
What

,

,

,

It

;

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

,
from

low
,

,

,

.
<EOS>
With

,

;

,

,

.
  
<EOS>
Those

,

,

,

,

:

.
  
<EOS>
The

from

Genoa
,
is

,
or

,

.
<EOS>
Though

,

;

,

<EOS>
A

;

.
<EOS>
A

,

<EOS>
If

,

intirely

,

,

,

,

,

-

<EOS>
If

,

<EOS>
This

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
A

<EOS>
I

<EOS>
In

<EOS>
These

<EOS>
For

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
E

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

When

wind
is
high
,
no
feluca
will
attempt
to
pass
it
;
even
in
a
moderate
breeze
,
the
waves
dashing
against
the
rocks
and
caverns
,
which
echo
with
the
sound
,
make
such
an
awful
noise
,
and
at
the
same
time
occasion
such
a
rough
sea
,
as
one
can
not
hear
,
and
see
,
and
feel
,
without
a
secret
horror
.
  
<EOS>
On
this
side
of
the
Cape
,
there
is
a
beautiful
strand
cultivated
like
a
garden
;
the
plantations
extend
to
the
very
tops
of
the
hills
,
interspersed
with
villages
,
castles
,
churches
,
and
villas
.
<EOS>
Indeed
the
whole
Riviera
is
ornamented
in
the
same
manner
,
except
in
such
places
as
admit
of
no
building
nor
 
cultivation
.
  
<EOS>
Having
passed
the
Cape
,
we
followed
the
winding
of
the
coast
,
into
a
small
bay
,
and
arrived
at
the
town
of
Noli
,
where
we
proposed
to
pass
the
night
.
<EOS>
You
will
be
surprised
that
we
did
not
go
ashore
sooner
,
in
order
to
take
some

;
but
the
truth

,
we
had
a
provision
of
ham
,

,
roasted

,
cheese
,
bread
,
wine
,
and

,
in
the
feluca
,
where
we
every

enjoyed
a
slight

about
one
or
two
o'clock
in

.
<EOS>
This
I
mention
as
a
necessary

information
to

same

.
<EOS>
We
likewise
found

to
lay

vie
,
or

,
for

,

.
 
<EOS>
On

,
however
,
those
ragamuffins
will
rather
die
of

than
suffer
the
least

.
<EOS>
I

frequently

,
by

something

,
on
a
Friday

:
but
they
always
declined

with

,
crying
,
Dio
me
<EOS>
ne
libere
!
<EOS>
God
deliver
me
from
it
!
<EOS>
or
some

words
to
that

.
<EOS>
I
moreover

,
that
not

those

ever
swore

,
or

.
<EOS>
They
would
by
no

to
sea
,
of

,

heard
mass
;
and

,

Blessed

,
or

,

.
<EOS>
I
have
indeed
remarked
all

,
that
a

the

of
the
church
in

,

.
<EOS>
A

,

,
or
s
 
m
 
te
,

from

,

;

,

<EOS>
I

;
and

,
that
a

-
warm

,
little

;
and

.
  
<EOS>
Noli
is
a
small

of
fishermen
subject
to
Genoa
;
but
very
tenacious

.
<EOS>
The

stands
on

beach
,
tolerably

,
defended
by
a
castle

on
a
rock

;
and

.
<EOS>
The
auberge
was
such
as
made

St
Remo
.
<EOS>
After

odd
kind

,
which

,
we

:
<EOS>
but

,

on
different

,

,

.
<EOS>
You

<EOS>
I

,

.
  
<EOS>
One

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

<EOS>
It

's

,

.
<EOS>
Next

,

,

<EOS>
Then

<EOS>
About

St
Pietro
d
'

,

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Turning

.
<EOS>
It

<EOS>

<EOS>
But

<EOS>
Within

<EOS>
We

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
NICE
,

.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
It
is
not
without
reason
that
Genoa
is
called
La
superba
.
<EOS>
The
city
itself
is
very
stately
;
and
the
nobles
are
very
proud
.
<EOS>
Some
few
of
them
may
be
proud
of
their
wealth
:
but
,
in
general
,
their
fortunes
are
very
small
.
<EOS>
My
friend
Mr
R
  
assured
me
that
many
Genoese
noblemen
had
fortunes
of
half
a
million
of
livres
per
annum
:
but
the
truth
is
,
the
whole
revenue
of
the
state
does
not
exceed
this

;
and
the
livre
of
Genoa
is
but
about
nine
pence
sterling
.
<EOS>
There
are
about
half
a
dozen
of
their
nobles
who
have
ten

a
year
:
but
the

have
not
above
a
twentieth

of
that
sum
.
<EOS>
They
live
with
great
parsimony
in
their
families
;
and
wear
nothing
but
black
in
public
;
so
that
their
expences
are
but
small
.
<EOS>
If

Genoese
nobleman
gives
an

once
a
quarter
,
he
is
said
to
live
upon
the
fragments
all
the
rest
of
the
year
.
<EOS>
I
was
told
that
one
of
them
lately
treated
his
friends
,
and
left
the

to
the
care
of
his
son
,
who
ordered
a
dish
of
fish
that
cost
a
zechine
,
which
is
equal
to
about
ten
shillings
sterling
.
<EOS>
The

no

it
appear

,
than

,

,

,
Ah
Figliuolo
indegno
!
<EOS>
Siamo
in
Rovina
!
<EOS>
Siamo
in
precipizio
!
<EOS>
Ah
,
Prodigal
!
<EOS>
ruined
!
<EOS>
undone
!
  
<EOS>
I
think
the
pride
or
ostentation
of
the
Italians
in

takes
a
more
laudable

than
that

other

.
<EOS>
A
Frenchman
lays

upon

,
or

,

.
<EOS>
His

to

;
his

,

to

,
and

.
<EOS>
A

,

,
keeps
himself

his

at
short

,
that

save

and

,
which
remain
to
after
-
ages
<EOS>
so
many

of
his

,

;

.
<EOS>
There

,

<EOS>
The

.
  
<EOS>
The

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Their

,

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>
True

<EOS>
For

,

<EOS>
"

<EOS>
Sestri

<EOS>

in

a
mile
farther
arrived
at
Sarzana
,
a
small
town
at
the
extremity
of
the
Genoese
territories
,
where
we
changed
horses
.
<EOS>
Then
entering
the
principalities
of
Massa
and
Carrara
,
belonging
to
the
duke
of
Modena
,
we
passed
Lavenza
,
which
seems
to
be
a
decayed
fort
with
a
small
garrison
,
and
dined
at
Massa
,
which
is
an
agreeable
little
town
,
where
the
old
dutchess
of
Modena
resides
.
<EOS>
Notwithstanding
all
the
expedition
we
could
make
,
it
was
dark
before
we
passed
the
Cerchio
,
which
is
an
inconsiderable
stream
in
the
neighbourhood
of
Pisa
,
where
we
arrived
about
eight
in
the
evening
.
  
<EOS>
The
country
from
Sarzana
to
the
frontiers
of
Tuscany
is
a
narrow
plain
,
bounded
on
the
right
by
the
sea
,
and
on
the
left
by
the
Apennine
mountains
.
<EOS>
It
is
well
cultivated
and
inclosed
,
consisting
of
meadow
-
ground
,
corn
fields
,
plantations
of
olives
;
and
the
trees
that
form
the
hedge
-
rows
serve
as
so
many
props
to
the
vines
,
which
are
twisted
round
them
,
and
continued
from
one
to
another
.
<EOS>
After
entering
the
dominions
of
Tuscany
,
we
travelled
through
a
noble
forest
of
oak
-
trees
of
a
considerable
extent
,
which
would
have
appeared
much
more
agreeable
,
had
we
not
been
benighted
and
apprehensive
of
robbers
.
<EOS>
The
last
post
but
one
in
this
days
journey
,
is
at
the
little
town
of
Viareggio
,
a
kind
of
sea
-
port
on
the
Mediterranean
,
belonging
to
Lucia
.
<EOS>
The
roads
are
indifferent
,
and
the
accommodation
is
execrable
.
<EOS>
I
was
glad
to
find
myself
housed
in
a
very
good
inn
at
Pisa
,
where
I
promised
myself
a
good
night
's
rest
,
and
was
not
disappointed
.
<EOS>
I
heartily
wish
you
the
same
pleasure
,
and
am
very
sincerely
 
Yours
.
   
<EOS>
NICE
,
January
28
,
1765
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
Pisa
is
a
fine
old
city
that
strikes
you
with
the
same
veneration
you
would
feel
at
sight
of
an
antient
temple
which
bears
the
marks
of
decay
,
without
being
absolutely
dilapidated
.
<EOS>
The
houses
are
well
built
,
the
streets
open
,
straight
,
and
well
paved
;
the
shops
well
furnished
;
and
the
markets
well
supplied
:
there
are
some
elegant
palaces
,
designed
by
great
masters
.
<EOS>
The
churches
are
built
with
taste
,
and
tolerably
ornamented
.
<EOS>
There
is
a
beautiful
wharf
of
freestone
on
each
side
of
the
river
Arno
,
which
runs
through
the
city
,
and
three
bridges
thrown
over
it
,
of
which
that
in
the
middle
is
of
marble
,
a
pretty
piece
of
architecture
:
but
the
number
of
inhabitants
is
very
inconsiderable
;
and
this
very
circumstance
gives
it
an
air
of
majestic
solitude
,
which
is
far
from
being
unpleasant
to
a
man
of
a
contemplative
turn
of
mind
.
<EOS>
For
my
part
,
I
can
not
bear
the
tumult
of
a
populous
commercial
city
;
and
the
solitude
that
reigns
in
Pisa
would
with
me
be
a
strong
motive
to
choose
it
as
a
place
of
residence
.
<EOS>
Not
that
this
would
be
the
only
inducement
for
living
at
Pisa
.
<EOS>
Here
is
some
good
company
,
and
even
a
few
men
of
taste
and
learning
.
<EOS>
The
people
in
general
are
counted
sociable
and
polite
;
and
there
is
great
plenty
of
provisions
,
at
a
very
reasonable
rate
.
<EOS>
At
some

from
the
more

of
the
city
,
a
man
may
hire
a
large
house
for
thirty
crowns
a
year
:
but
near
the
center
,
you
can
not
have
good
lodgings
,
ready
furnished
,
for
less
than
a
scudo
(
about
five

)
a
day
.
<EOS>
The
air
in
summer
is
reckoned
unwholesome
by
the
exhalations
arising
from
stagnant
water
in
the
neighbourhood
of
the
city
,
which
stands
in
the
midst
of
a
fertile
plain
,
low
and
marshy
:
yet
these

have
been
considerably

,
and
the
air

.
<EOS>
As

Arno
,
it

.
<EOS>
The
university
of
Pisa
is
very
much

;
and
except
the
little
business
occasioned
by
the
emperor
's

,
which
are

in
this

,
[
This

.
<EOS>
No

have

here
for

great
many
years
,
and
the
dock
is
now
converted
into

for
the
Grand
Duke
's

.
]
<EOS>
I
know
of
no
commerce
it
carried
on
:
perhaps
the

live
on
the
produce
of
the
country
,
which

of
corn
,

,

.
<EOS>
They

,

;

<EOS>
This

,

,

<EOS>
You
need
not
doubt
<EOS>
but

,
or
hanging
-

,
which

,
<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
For

<EOS>

<EOS>
I

[
All

Building

Inclination

]
<EOS>

,

.
<EOS>
These

;

<EOS>
In

,

pile
,
[
This
Edifice

.
<EOS>
It

<EOS>
The

,

<EOS>

<EOS>
I

<EOS>
In

<EOS>

<EOS>
Within

<EOS>
It

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Though

<EOS>
[

.
]

Here

equally

For

<EOS>
DEAR

<EOS>

is
,
at
best
,
no
other
than
a
monster
in
nature
,
which
I
never
had
any
pleasure
in
viewing
:
nor
,
indeed
,
do
I
think
there
was
much
talent
required
in
representing
a
figure
with
the
head
and
breasts
of
a
woman
,
and
all
the
other
parts
of
the
body
masculine
.
<EOS>
There
is
such
a
profusion
of
curiosities
in
this
celebrated
musaeum
;
statues
,
busts
,
pictures
,
medals
,
tables
inlaid
in
the
way
of
marquetry
,
<EOS>
cabinets
adorned
with
precious
stones
,
jewels
of
all
sorts
,
mathematical
instruments
,
antient
arms
and
military
machines
,
that
the
imagination
is
bewildered
,
and
a
stranger
of
a
visionary
turn
,
would
be
apt
to
fancy
himself
in
a
palace
of
the
fairies
,
raised
and
adorned
by
the
power
of
inchantment
.
  
<EOS>
In
one
of
the
detached
apartments
,
I
saw
the
antependium
of
the
altar
,
designed
for
the
famous
chapel
of
St
Lorenzo
.
<EOS>
It
is
a
curious
piece
of
architecture
,
inlaid
with
coloured
marble
and
precious
stones
,
so
as
to
represent
an
infinite
variety
of
natural
objects
.
<EOS>
It
is
adorned
with
some
crystal
pillars
,
with
capitals
of
beaten
gold
.
<EOS>
The
second
story
of
the
building
is
occupied
by
a
great
number
of
artists
employed
in
this
very
curious
work
of
marquetry
,
representing
figures
with
gems
and
different
kinds
of
coloured
marble
,
for
the
use
of
the
emperor
.
<EOS>
The
Italians
call
it
pietre
commesse
,
a
sort
of
inlaying
with
stones
,
analogous
to
the
fineering
of
cabinets
in
wood
.
<EOS>
It
is
peculiar
to
Florence
,
and
seems
to
be
still
more
curious
than
the
Mosaic
work
,
which
the
Romans
have
brought
to
great
perfection
.
  
<EOS>
The
cathedral
of
Florence
is
a
great
Gothic
building
,
encrusted
on
the
outside
with
marble
;
it
is
remarkable
for
nothing
but
its
cupola
,
which
is
said
to
have
been
copied
by
the
architect
of
St
Peter
's
at
Rome
,
and
for
its
size
,
which
is
much
greater
than
that
of
any
other
church
in
Christendom
.
 
<EOS>
[
In
this
cathedral
is
the
Tomb
of
Johannes
<EOS>
Acutus
Anglus
,
which
a
man
would
naturally
interpret
as
John
Sharp
;
but
his
name
was
really
Hawkwood
,
which
the
Italians
have
corrupted
into
Acut
.
<EOS>
He
was
a
celebrated
General
or
Condottiere
who
arrived
in
Italy
at
the
head
of
four
thousand
soldiers
of
fortune
,
mostly
Englishmen
who
had
served
with
him
in
the
army
of
King
Edward
III
,
and
were
dismissed
at
the
Peace
of
Bontigny
.
<EOS>
Hawkwood
greatly
distinguished
himself
in
Italy
by
his
valour
and
conduct
,
and
died
a
very
old
man
in
the
Florentine
service
.
<EOS>
He
was
the
son
of
a
Tanner
in
Essex
,
and
had
been
put
apprentice
to
a
Taylor
.
]
<EOS>
The
baptistery
,
which
stands
by
it
,
was
an
antient
temple
,
said
to
be
dedicated
to
Mars
.
<EOS>
There
are
some
good
statues
of
marble
within
;
and
one
or
two
of
bronze
on
the
outside
of
the
doors
;
but
it
is
chiefly
celebrated
for
the
embossed
work
of
its
brass
gates
,
by
Lorenzo
Ghiberti
,
which
Buonaroti
used
to
say
,
deserved
to
be
made
the
gates
of
Paradise
.
<EOS>
I
viewed
them
with
pleasure
:
<EOS>
but
still
I
retained
a
greater
veneration
for
those
of
Pisa
,
which
I
had
first
admired
:
a
preference
which
either
arises
from
want
of
taste
,
or
from
the
charm
of
novelty
,
by
which
the
former
were
recommended
to
my
attention
.
<EOS>
Those
who
would
have
a
particular
detail
of
every
thing
worth
seeing
at
Florence
,
comprehending
churches
,
libraries
,
palaces
,
tombs
,
statues
,
pictures
,
fountains
,
bridge
,
etc
.
may
consult
Keysler
,
who
is
so
laboriously
circumstantial
in
his
descriptions
,
that
I
never
could
peruse
them
,
without
suffering
the
headache
,
and
recollecting
the
old
observation
,
that
the
German
genius
lies
more
in
the
back
than
in
the
brain
.
  
<EOS>
I
was
much
disappointed
in
the
chapel
of
St
Lorenzo
.
<EOS>
Notwithstanding
the
great
profusion
of
granite
,
porphyry
,
jasper
,
verde
antico
,
lapis
-
lazuli
,
and
other
precious
stones
,
representing
figures
in
the
way
of
marquetry
,
I
think
the
whole
has
a
gloomy
effect
.
<EOS>
These
pietre
commesse
are
better
calculated
for
cabinets
,
than
for
ornaments
to
great
buildings
,
which
ought
to
be
large
masses
proportioned
to
the
greatness
of
the
edifice
.
<EOS>
The
compartments
are
so
small
,
that
they
produce
no
effect
in
giving
the
first
impression
when
one
enters
the
place
;
except
to
give
an
air
of
littleness
to
the
whole
,
just
as
if
a
grand
saloon
was
covered
with
pictures
painted
in
miniature
.
<EOS>
If
they
have
as
little
regard
to
proportion
and
perspective
,
when
they
paint
the
dome
,
which
is
not
yet
finished
,
this
chapel
will
,
in
my
opinion
,
remain
a
monument
of
ill
taste
and
extravagance
.
  
<EOS>
The
court
of
the
palace
of
Pitti
is
formed
by
three
sides
of
an
elegant
square
,
with
arcades
all
round
,
like
the
palace
of
Holyrood
house
at
Edinburgh
;
and
the
rustic
work
,
which
constitutes
the
lower
part
of
the
building
,
gives
it
an
air
of
strength
and
magnificence
.
<EOS>
In
this
court
,
there
is
a
fine
fountain
,
in
which
the
water
trickles
down
from
above
;
and
here
is
also
an
admirable
antique
statue
of
Hercules
,
inscribed
LUSIPPOI
ERGON
,
the
work
of
Lysippus
.
  
<EOS>
The
apartments
of
this
palace
are
generally
small
,
and
many
of
them
dark
.
<EOS>
Among
the
paintings
the
most
remarkable
is
the
Madonna
de
la
Seggiola
,
by
Raphael
,
counted
one
of
the
best
coloured
pieces
of
that
great
master
.
<EOS>
If
I
was
allowed
to
find
fault
with
the
performance
,
I
should
pronounce
it
defective
in
dignity
and
sentiment
.
<EOS>
It
is
the
expression
of
a
peasant
rather
than
of
the
mother
of
God
.
<EOS>
She
exhibits
the
fondness
and
joy
of
a
young
woman
towards
her
firstborn
son
,
without
that
rapture
of
admiration
which
we
expect
to
find
in
the
Virgin
Mary
,
while
she
contemplates
,
in
the
fruit
of
her
own
womb
,
the
Saviour
of
mankind
.
<EOS>
In
other
respects
,
it
is
a
fine
figure
,
gay
,
agreeable
,
and
very
expressive
of
maternal
tenderness
;
and
the
bambino
is
extremely
beautiful
.
<EOS>
There
was
an
English
painter
employed
in
copying
this
picture
,
and
what
he
had
done
was
executed
with
great
success
.
<EOS>
I
am
one
of
those
who
think
it
very
possible
to
imitate
the
best
pieces
in
such
a
manner
,
that
even
the
connoisseurs
shall
not
be
able
to
distinguish
the
original
from
the
copy
.
<EOS>
After
all
,
I
do
not
set
up
for
a
judge
in
these
matters
,
and
very
likely
I
may
incur
the
ridicule
of
the
virtuosi
for
the
remarks
I
have
made
:
but
I
am
used
to
speak
my
mind
freely
on
all
subjects
that
fall
under
the
cognizance
of
my
senses
;
though
I
must
as
freely
own
,
there
is
something
more
than
common
sense
required
to
discover
and
distinguish
the
more
delicate
beauties
of
painting
.
<EOS>
I
can
safely
say
,
however
,
that
without
any
daubing
at
all
,
I
am
,
very
sincerely
 
Your
affectionate
humble
servant
.
   
<EOS>
NICE
,
February
20
,
1765
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
Having
seen
all
the
curiosities
of
Florence
,
and
hired
a
good
travelling
coach
for
seven
weeks
,
at
the
price
of
seven
zequines
,
something
less
than
three
guineas
and
a
half
,
we
set
out
post
for
Rome
,
by
the
way
of
Sienna
,
where
we
lay
the
first
night
.
<EOS>
The
country
through
which
we
passed
is
mountainous
but
agreeable
.
<EOS>
Of
Sienna
I
can
say
nothing
from
my
own
observation
,
but
that
we
were
indifferently
lodged
in
a
house
that
stunk
like
a
privy
,
and
fared
wretchedly
at
supper
.
<EOS>
The
city
is
large
and
well
built
:
the
inhabitants
pique
themselves
upon
their
politeness
,
and
the
purity
of
their
dialect
.
<EOS>
Certain
it
is
,
some
strangers
reside
in
this
place
on
purpose
to
learn
the
best
pronunciation
of
the
Italian
tongue
.
<EOS>
The
Mosaic
pavement
of
their
duomo
,
or
cathedral
,
has
been
much
admired
;
as
well
as
the
history
of
Aeneas
Sylvius
,
afterwards
pope
Pius
II
,
painted
on
the
walls
of
the
library
,
partly
by
Pietro
Perugino
,
and
partly
by
his
pupil
Raphael
D'Urbino
.
  
<EOS>
Next
day
,
at
Buon
Convento
,
where
the
emperor
Henry
VII
was
poisoned
by
a
friar
with
the
sacramental
wafer
,
I
refused
to
give
money
to
the
hostler
,
who
in
revenge
put
two
young
unbroke
stone
-
horses
in
the
traces
next
to
the
coach
,
which
became
so
unruly
,
that
before
we
had
gone
a
quarter
of
a
mile
,
they
and
the
postilion
were
rolling
in
the
dust
.
<EOS>
In
this
situation
they
made
such
efforts
to
disengage
themselves
,
and
kicked
with
such
violence
,
that
I
imagined
the
carriage
and
all
our
trunks
would
have
been
beaten
in
pieces
.
<EOS>
We
leaped
out
of
the
coach
,
however
,
without
sustaining
any
personal
damage
,
except
the
fright
;
nor
was
any
hurt
done
to
the
vehicle
.
<EOS>
But
the
horses
were
terribly
bruised
,
and
almost
strangled
,
before
they
could
be
disengaged
.
<EOS>
Exasperated
at
the
villany
of
the
hostler
,
I
resolved
to
make
a
complaint
to
the
uffiziale
or
magistrate
of
the
place
.
<EOS>
I
found
him
wrapped
in
an
old
,
greasy
,
ragged
,
great
-
coat
,
sitting
in
a
wretched
apartment
,
without
either
glass
,
paper
,
or
boards
in
the
windows
;
and
there
was
no
sort
of
furniture
but
a
couple
of
broken
chairs
and
a
miserable
truckle
-
bed
.
<EOS>
He
looked
pale
,
and
meagre
,
and
had
more
the
air
of
a
half
-
starved
prisoner
than
of
a
magistrate
.
<EOS>
Having
heard
my
complaint
,
he
came
forth
into
a
kind
of
outward
room
or
bellfrey
,
and
rung
a
great
bell
with
his
own
hand
.
<EOS>
In
consequence
of
this
signal
,
the
postmaster
came
up
stairs
,
and
I
suppose
he
was
the
first
man
in
the
place
,
for
the
uffiziale
stood
before
him
cap
-
in
-
hand
,
and
with
great
marks
of
humble
respect
repeated
the
complaint
I
had
made
.
<EOS>
This
man
assured
me
,
with
an
air
of
conscious
importance
,
that
he
himself
had
ordered
the
hostler
to
supply
me
with
those
very
horses
,
which
were
the
best
in
his
stable
;
and
that
the
misfortune
which
happened
was
owing
to
the
misconduct
of
the
fore
-
postilion
,
who
did
not
keep
the
fore
-
horses
to
a
proper
speed
proportioned
to
the
mettle
of
the
other
two
.
<EOS>
As
he
took
the
affair
upon
himself
,
and
I
perceived
had
an
ascendancy
over
the
magistrate
,
I
contented
myself
with
saying
,
I
was
certain
the
two
horses
had
been
put
to
the
coach
on
purpose
,
either
to
hurt
or
frighten
us
;
and
that
since
I
could
not
have
justice
here
I
would
make
a
formal
complaint
to
the
British
minister
at
Florence
.
<EOS>
In
passing
through
the
street
to
the
coach
,
which
was
by
this
time
furnished
with
fresh
horses
,
I
met
the
hostler
,
and
would
have
caned
him
heartily
;
but
perceiving
my
intention
,
he
took
to
his
heels
and
vanished
.
<EOS>
Of
all
the
people
I
have
ever
seen
,
the
hostlers
,
postilions
,
and
other
fellows
hanging
about
the
post
-
houses
in
Italy
,
are
the
most
greedy
,
impertinent
,
and
provoking
.
<EOS>
Happy
are
those
travellers
who
have
phlegm
enough
to
disregard
their
insolence
and
importunity
:
for
this
is
not
so
disagreeable
as
their
revenge
is
dangerous
.
<EOS>
An
English
gentleman
at
Florence
told
me
,
that
one
of
those
fellows
,
whom
he
had
struck
for
his
impertinence
,
flew
at
him
with
a
long
knife
,
and
he
could
hardly
keep
him
at
sword
's
point
.
<EOS>
All
of
them
wear
such
knives
,
and
are
very
apt
to
use
them
on
the
slightest
provocation
.
<EOS>
But
their
open
attacks
are
not
so
formidable
as
their
premeditated
schemes
of
revenge
;
in
the
prosecution
of
which
the
Italians
are
equally
treacherous
and
cruel
.
  
<EOS>
This
night
we
passed
at
a
place
called
Radicofani
,
a
village
and
fort
,
situated
on
the
top
of
a
very
high
mountain
.
<EOS>
The
inn
stands
still
lower
than
the
town
.
<EOS>
It
was
built
at
the
expence
of
the
last
grand
-
duke
of
Tuscany
;
is
very
large
,
very
cold
,
and
uncomfortable
.
<EOS>
One
would
imagine
it
was
contrived
for
coolness
,
though
situated
so
high
,
that
even
in
the
midst
of
summer
,
a
traveller
would
be
glad
to
have
a
fire
in
his
chamber
.
<EOS>
But
few
,
or
none
of
them
have
fireplaces
,
and
there
is
not
a
bed
with
curtains
or
tester
in
the
house
.
<EOS>
All
the
adjacent
country
is
naked
and
barren
.
<EOS>
On
the
third
day
we
entered
the
pope
's
territories
,
some
parts
of
which
are
delightful
.
<EOS>
Having
passed
Aqua
-
Pendente
,
a
beggarly
town
,
situated
on
the
top
of
a
rock
,
from
whence
there
is
a
romantic
cascade
of
water
,
which
gives
it
the
name
,
we
travelled
along
the
side
of
the
lake
Bolsena
,
a
beautiful
piece
of
water
about
thirty
miles
in
circuit
,
with
two
islands
in
the
middle
,
the
banks
covered
with
noble
plantations
of
oak

<EOS>
Nothing

,

<EOS>

antient

<EOS>
The

or

built
by

,
is

.
<EOS>
Near

,

,

<EOS>

.
<EOS>

;

<EOS>
The

,

.
<EOS>

<EOS>

and
miserable
condition
,
that
Mrs
Vanini
almost
fainted

sight

,
on

supposition

had
met

some

,

that

.
<EOS>
My

were
immediately

,

,

,
arising

,

a

my

:
<EOS>
not

expected
a

,

a

:
<EOS>
but

.
<EOS>
I
now

to
the

Barazzi
,
fully

that

,

.
<EOS>
In

,

of

,

of
the

,

,

,

in

.
  
<EOS>
I

,

:

C
  
and

R
 
,

,

of

,

by

,

.
<EOS>
Mr
R
  
seemed

be
much

;
but
,

did
not

no

give

for

.
<EOS>
They

been
exposed
to
a

from
the

of
the
road
.
<EOS>
The

had
been
several

in

most
imminent

of
being
lost
with
all

;
and

,

as

,

.
<EOS>
It

in

,
that
the

his

,
Mr
R
  
and
my

,
had
like

gone
all

.
<EOS>
The

of
Miss
C
 
,
who

from
cold

<EOS>
The

,
when
all

.
<EOS>
I

,
who
must
by
this

quite
jaded
with
this

,
which

closed
without

by
,
 
Yours
always
.
   
<EOS>
NICE
,
March
20
,
1765
.
  
<EOS>
DEAR
SIR
,
 
<EOS>
The
season
being
far
advanced
,
and
the
weather
growing

,
I
made
but

short

at
Florence
,
and
set

for
Pisa
,
with
full

to
take
the

to
Lerici
,
where

proposed
to
hire
a

for

.
<EOS>
I

a
great

to
see
Leghorn

;
but

a
winter
's

by
sea
in
an
open

effectually

.
<EOS>
To

of
having
our

shifted
every

,

hired
two
chaises
to
Pisa
for
a

,
and

arrived
in

about
seven

,

fear

the
consequence
,
as
the
calesses
were
quite
open
,
and
it
rained
all
the
way
.
<EOS>
I
must
own
<EOS>
I
was
so
sick
of
the
wretched
accommodation
<EOS>
one
meets
with
in
every
part
of
Italy
,
except
the
great

,
so
averse
to
the
sea
at

,

the

Pisa
,

,
<EOS>
had

,

;

's

.
<EOS>
I

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
When

,

,

,
which
the

formerly

,

.
<EOS>
It

;

,

seen

hurried

by

<EOS>
Near

<EOS>
The

between

Santa

.
<EOS>
When

Massa
,

,

,

.
<EOS>
We

,

.
<EOS>
Next

,

as

:

,

,

.
<EOS>
There

by

,

,

,

;

,

;

,

,

.
<EOS>
We

in

,

;

,

<EOS>
Here

<EOS>
An

,

.
<EOS>
He

.
<EOS>
This

,

,

English
;
his

who

,

my

,

in
coming

France
,

<EOS>
This

.
<EOS>
At

,

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
He

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>
